The Accident Cannot Subsist in Two Loci: A Self-Evident Truth
Al-Jurjani argues that one accident cannot subsist in two loci as a necessary judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the impossibility of a single accident subsisting in two loci is a self-evident judgment, supported by an argument from individuation.

Core Idea
One accident cannot be in two places—not deduced, but known by necessity.

In this section, al-Jurjani addresses the eighth objective: a single accident cannot exist in two different loci simultaneously. He insists that this is not a conclusion derived from reasoning, but a necessary judgment that the intellect knows immediately. The passage contrasts this with the analogous case of one body not being in two places, and provides a supportive argument based on the individuation of accidents by their loci.

Guided Path
1
Read the core claim and its necessity
Read the first part of the passage where al-Jurjani states that the accident does not subsist in two loci by necessity. Notice how he emphasizes that this is a judgment known without reflection.
The word 'ضرورة' indicates the self-evident nature.
2
Understand the comparison with body in place
Al-Jurjani compares this to the impossibility of one body being in two places. Both are self-evident. Consider why he makes this comparison and how it strengthens the claim.
He rejects a potential disanalogy: multiple accidents can coexist in one locus, but two bodies cannot.
3
Examine the supportive argument from individuation
Read the part where he says an accident is individuated by its locus. If one accident were in two loci, it would have two individuations, making it two things. This is impossible.
This is not a proof but a 'tayeed' (support) that explains the reason behind the necessity.
4
Consider the glosses clarifying the argument
The passage includes glosses that clarify that the intellect's judgment is intuitive, and that the supportive argument is based on the impossibility of two complete causes concurring on one effect.
Reflect on why each locus is considered a complete cause for the accident's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثامن : العرض لا يقوم بمحلين كذلك الحكم ببقاء الأعراض كالألوان ضروري يحكم به العقل بمعونته أيضاً والطرق المذكورة تنبيهات على حكم ضروري فالمناقشة فيها لا تجدي طائلا (العرض) الواحد بالشخص ) لا يقوم بمحلين ضرورة ) أي هذا حكم معلوم بالضرورة ولذلك نجزم بأن السواد القائم بهذا المحل غير السواد ( القائم بالمحل الآخر) جزماً يقينياً لا نحتاج فيه إلى فكر ) ولا فرق بينه ) أي بين جزمنا بأن العرض الواحد لا يقوم بمحلين ( وبين جزمنا بأن الجسم الواحد ( لا يوجد ) في آن واحد ( في مكانين فكما أنَّ الجزم الثاني بديهي بلا شبهة فكذا الأول ولسنا نقول نسبة العرض إلى المحل كنسبة الجسم إلى المكان فلو جاز حلوله في محلين لجاز حصول الجسم في مكانين حتى يرد عليه أنَّ النسبتين ليستا على السواء لا مكان حلول أعراض متعددة معاً في محل واحد وامتناع اجتماع جسمين في مكان واحد ( ويؤيده ) أي يؤيد ما ذكرناه من أن العرض يمتنع أن يقوم بمحلين ( أنَّ العرض إنما يتعين) ويتشخص ) بمحله ( كما مر . فلو قام عرض واحد بمحلين لكان له بحسب كل محل تعين وتشخص لامتناع توارد العلتين على شخص واحد وإذا كان له تعينان كان الواحد اثنين وهو محال وليس هذا استدلالاً لأن الحكم ضروري بل هو تأييد له ببيان لميته ( فإنَّ الشيء ) المعلوم بالبديهة ( إذا علم بلميته اطمأن إليه النفس أكثر ) الحس . قوله : ( يحكم به العقل إلخ قد عرفت أن حكم العقل ببقائها بديهي من غير معونة من قوله : ( أي هذا حكم معلوم بالضرورة يعني أن قوله : ضرورة متعلق بما يستفاد مما قبله أي يحكم به ضرورة لا بقوله لا يقوم . قوله : ( لامتناع توارد إلخ فإنَّ كل محل مع جميع ما يتوقف عليه وجود العرض علة تامة له فيلزم توارد المستقلين. قوله : ( أكثر ) أي مما يعلم بلميته . قوله : ( لامتناع توارد العلتين على شخص واحد) وهذا لازم على ذلك التقرير لأن كل واحد من المحلين وإن لم يكن علة مستقلة لكنه مع جميع ما يتوقف عليه ذلك الشخص علة تامة له لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يكون مجموع المحلين علة واحدة لتشخص ذلك العرض القائم بكل منهما لأنا نقول : لأنه يلزم حينئذ أن لا يوجد ذلك التشخص في واحد من المحلين لأنَّ في كل منهما إنما وجد جزء العلة دون تمامها وهو لا يوجد وجود المعلول فيلزم أن لا يوجد .
The First Observation – The Eighth Objective: The accident does not subsist in two loci. Similarly, the judgment of the persistence of accidents like colors is necessary; the intellect judges it with its aid as well, and the mentioned methods are reminders of a necessary judgment, so disputing them is of no avail. (The accident, one in person, does not subsist in two loci necessarily) i.e., this is a judgment known by necessity, and therefore we judge with certainty that the blackness subsisting in this locus is other than the blackness (subsisting in the other locus), a certain judgment for which we need no reflection. And there is no difference between (i.e., between our judgment that one accident does not subsist in two loci) and our judgment that one body (does not exist) in one instant (in two places). Just as the second judgment is self-evident without doubt, so is the first. And we do not say that the relation of the accident to the locus is like the relation of the body to place, for if its inherence in two loci were permissible, then the body's occurrence in two places would be permissible, until it is objected that the two relations are not equivalent, because multiple accidents can inhere simultaneously in one locus, while it is impossible for two bodies to occupy one place. (And it supports it) i.e., supports what we have mentioned that the accident is impossible to subsist in two loci (that the accident is only determined) and individuated (by its locus), as has passed. So if one accident were to subsist in two loci, it would have, according to each locus, a determination and individuation, because it is impossible for two causes to bear upon one individual. And if it has two determinations, then the one would be two, which is impossible. And this is not a proof, because the judgment is necessary, rather it is a confirmation of it by explaining its reason (why-ness). (For a thing) known by intuition, if it is known by its reason, the soul is more reassured by it. The sense. His statement: (The intellect judges it etc.) You have known that the intellect's judgment of their persistence is intuitive without aid from sense. His statement: (i.e., this is a judgment known by necessity) means that his statement 'necessarily' is related to what is understood from what precedes, i.e., he judges it necessarily, not by his statement 'does not subsist'. His statement: (Because of the impossibility of the concurrence of two causes upon one individual) for each locus, with all that the existence of the accident depends on, is a complete cause for it, so the concurrence of independent causes would occur. His statement: (More) i.e., than what is known by its reason. His statement: (Because of the impossibility of the concurrence of two causes upon one individual) And this is necessitated by that explanation, because each of the two loci, even if it is not an independent cause, yet with all that the individual depends on, it is a complete cause for it. It cannot be said: Why is it not permissible for the sum of the two loci to be one cause for the individuation of that accident subsisting in each of them? Because we say: It would then necessitate that that individuation does not exist in either of the two loci, because in each of them only part of the cause exists without its completion, and that does not produce the existence of the effect, so it would necessarily not exist.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage argues for the eighth objective: that one accident cannot exist simultaneously in two different loci. The author begins by comparing this to the necessity of the persistence of accidents, noting that both are necessary judgments that the intellect makes, and that disputing the methods mentioned is futile. He then states the core claim: a single accident (e.g., blackness in one particular body) cannot subsist in two loci by necessity. This is known intuitively, just as we know that one body cannot be in two places at once. He rejects a potential disanalogy: the relation of accident to locus is not like that of body to place, because multiple accidents can coexist in one locus but two bodies cannot occupy the same place; however, this does not affect the self-evident nature of the judgment. He then provides a supportive argument (not a proof, since the judgment is necessary): an accident is individuated by its locus. If one accident were in two loci, it would have two individuations, making it two things, which is impossible. This is a confirmation by explaining the reason (limee) for the necessity. The subsequent glosses clarify that the intellect's judgment is intuitive and does not require sensory aid; that the word 'necessarily' modifies the judgment, not the verb; and that the supportive argument relies on the fact that each locus is a complete cause for the accident's existence and individuation, so two complete causes would concur on one effect, which is impossible. The gloss also addresses an objection: why not consider the two loci together as one cause? The answer is that then neither locus alone would have the full cause, so the accident's individuation would not exist in either, leading to non-existence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of an accident subsisting in two loci is a conclusion derived from reasoning.
Correction: The passage emphasizes it is known by necessity, and the argument provided is only a supportive explanation (tayeed) not a proof.

Checkpoint
What is the main basis for the claim that an accident cannot subsist in two loci according to the passage?
A) It is proved by the analogy with bodies in places.
B) It is known by necessity, like the fact that one body cannot be in two places.
C) It follows from the impossibility of two causes concurring on one effect.
D) It is derived from the definition of an accident.

Key Takeaway
The judgment that one accident cannot subsist in two loci is self-evident and does not require proof; the argument from individuation merely confirms it.

The passage says that knowing the reason (لميته) behind a self-evident truth makes the soul more reassured. What is one belief you hold that would benefit from understanding its underlying reason? Will you actively seek that understanding this week?

The Argument from Individuation: Accident is Individualized by Its Locus
Al-Jurjani presents a supporting argument (ta'yid) for the impossibility of one accident in two loci.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that an accident is individuated by its locus and why it serves as a confirmation (ta'yid) rather than a proof (istidlal).

Core Idea
Accidents are individuated by their locus; one accident cannot have two loci without becoming two accidents.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents a supporting argument for the impossibility of one accident in two loci. He clarifies that this is not a proof but an explanation of the 'why' behind a necessary judgment. Let's examine the logic and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then follow with the literal translation. Notice the structure: the author states his support, gives the argument, and then explains its nature.
Pay attention to the terms تعين (determination) and تشخص (individuation).
2
Identify the core argument
The argument states that an accident is individuated by its locus. If one accident had two loci, it would have two individuations, making it two things, which is impossible.
Reflect on why this impossibility is considered necessary.
3
Understand the role of ta'yid vs. istidlal
The author explicitly says this is not a proof but a support that explains the 'why' (limiyya) of a necessary judgment. This clarifies the epistemological status of the argument.
Consider why the author distinguishes between a proof and a support.
4
Examine the commentator's expansion
The commentator explains that each locus, together with all that the accident's existence depends on, is a complete cause. Thus, two loci would require two independent causes for the same accident, which is impossible.
This reinforces the argument by showing the causal implication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويؤيده ) أي يؤيد ما ذكرناه من أن العرض يمتنع أن يقوم بمحلين ( أنَّ العرض إنما يتعين) ويتشخص ) بمحله ( كما مر . فلو قام عرض واحد بمحلين لكان له بحسب كل محل تعين وتشخص لامتناع توارد العلتين على شخص واحد وإذا كان له تعينان كان الواحد اثنين وهو محال وليس هذا استدلالاً لأن الحكم ضروري بل هو تأييد له ببيان لميته ( فإنَّ الشيء ) المعلوم بالبديهة ( إذا علم بلميته اطمأن إليه النفس أكثر ) قوله : ( لامتناع توارد إلخ فإنَّ كل محل مع جميع ما يتوقف عليه وجود العرض علة تامة له فيلزم توارد المستقلين. قوله : ( أكثر ) أي مما يعلم بلميته .
And it supports [what we have mentioned] that the accident cannot subsist in two loci — that the accident is only determined and individuated by its locus, as previously stated. So if one accident subsisted in two loci, it would have, according to each locus, a determination and individuation, because it is impossible for two causes to bear upon one individual. And if it has two individuations, the one would become two, which is impossible. And this is not a proof (istidlal) because the judgment is necessary, but rather it is a support (ta'yid) for it by explaining its 'why' (limiyya). For a thing known by intuition, when its 'why' is known, the mind is more at ease with it. The commentator said: 'The phrase "because it is impossible for two causes to bear" etc. For each locus, together with all that the existence of the accident depends on, is a complete cause for it, thus requiring the concurrence of two independent causes.' And he said: 'The phrase "more" means more than what is known by its why.'
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents a supporting argument for the impossibility of one accident in two loci. It is not a proof, but a confirmation (ta'yid) that explains the 'why' behind the necessary judgment. The argument: accidents are individuated by their loci. If one accident had two loci, it would have two individuations, making it two things, which is absurd. This shows that the impossibility is grounded in the nature of accidents. The commentator expands on the notion that each locus, with its conditions, is a complete cause for the accident's existence, so having two loci would require two independent causes for the same effect, which is impossible. This is a confirmation using causal explanation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is a proof (istidlal) for the impossibility.
Correction: The author explicitly states it is only a support (ta'yid) that explains the 'why' of a necessary judgment, not a proof.

Checkpoint
Why is the argument from individuation considered a support (ta'yid) rather than a proof (istidlal)?
A) Because it is weaker
B) Because the judgment is necessary and needs no proof
C) Because it uses analogy
D) Because it relies on scripture

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of one accident in two loci is a necessary judgment, and the argument from individuation confirms it by explaining its 'why'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator says each locus, together with all that the accident depends on, is a complete cause. Think of an intention you had this week: what ‘locus’ individuated it? Could that same intention be shared between pleasing Allah and seeking praise? What does this argument say about the impossibility of one cause having two competing ends? How will you protect the singularity of your intention in your next act?
?
The author notes that knowing the ‘why’ gives the mind ease. Is there a belief you hold by intuition (like Allah’s unity) for which you have not yet sought a rational explanation? What stops you from seeking that understanding? How might this lesson’s approach of seeking causal explanation strengthen your certainty without replacing it?
?
If an accident cannot be in two loci, consider your own heart: can it truly be devoted to two ultimate goals simultaneously? Reflect on a time you tried to serve both worldly gain and the Hereafter in the same act. How does the argument from individuation expose the contradiction in that attempt? What one locus will you choose to ground your actions today?

The Impossibility of Composition in More Than Two Substances
Main argument against composition in three parts and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that a single composition cannot subsist in more than two substances and the response that distinguishes between numerically different compositions.

Core Idea
Composition in two and three parts are numerically distinct accidents.

Abu Hashim argues that composition cannot subsist in more than two substances. He presents a proof by contradiction and then addresses an objection that might arise. This lesson walks through the argument and its answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the structure: a claim, a proof, an objection, and an answer.
The argument uses a conditional: 'if... then...'
2
Understand the objection
The objection claims that if composition subsisted in three, removing one would destroy composition, but the two remaining parts still have composition. Why is this a problem?
This shows that the original composition cannot be the same as the one in the two parts.
3
Grasp the answer
The answer distinguishes between two compositions: one subsisting in two parts and another in three. They are numerically different.
So when one part is removed, only the three-part composition perishes, not the two-part one.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why this distinction is necessary and how it saves Abu Hashim's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني وهو أنه لا يقوم بأكثر من جوهرين فلانه لو قام التأليف الواحد بثلاثة أجزاء مثلاً لعدم التأليف بعدم جزء واحد من تلك الثلاثة لأن عدم المحل يستلزم عدم الحال فيه والتالي باطل لأن الجزأين الباقيين بينهما تأليف قطعاً لأن صعوبة الانفكاك باقية بينهما وجوابه أن التأليف الذي بين الجزأين غير التأليف الذي بين الثلاثة أي يجوز أن يقوم تأليف واحد الجزأين كما ذكرته ويقوم تأليف آخر بثلاثة أجزاء فيكون هذا التأليف القائم بالثلاثة مغايراً بالشخص للتأليف الأول القائم بالجزأين وإن ماثله في الحقيقة النوعية والمنفي عند ما عدم واحد من الثلاثة هو التأليف الثاني القائم بالثلاثة دون التأليف الأول القائم بالاثنين فلا يلزم حينئذ انعدام التأليف بينهما واعلم أن العرض الواحد بالشخص يجوز قيامه بمحل منقسم بحيث ينقسم ذلك العرض بانقسامه حتى يوجد كل جزء منه في جزء من محله فهذا مما لا نزاع فيه وقيامه بمحل منقسم على وجه لا ينقسم بانقسام محله مختلف فيه كما سيأتي وأما قيامه بمحل مع قيامه بعينه بمحل آخر فهو الذي ذكرنا أنَّ بطلانه بديهي وما نقل عن أبي هاشم في التأليف إن حمل على القسم الأول فلا منازعة معه إلافي انقسام التأليف، وكونه وجودياً وإن حمل على القسم الثاني فبعد تسليم جوازه تبقى المناقشة في وجودية التأليف والمشهور أنَّ مراده القسم الثالث الذي علم بطلانه بديهة .
And as for the second [part], which is that it does not subsist in more than two substances, it is because if a single composition subsisted in three parts, for example, composition would be absent by the absence of one part of those three, because the non-existence of the locus necessitates the non-existence of the accident therein. But the consequent is false, because the two remaining parts certainly have composition between them, since the difficulty of disjunction remains between them. Its answer: that the composition which is between the two parts is other than the composition which is between the three; i.e., it is permissible that a single composition subsists in two parts, as you mentioned, and another composition subsists in three parts. So this composition subsisting in the three is numerically different from the first composition subsisting in the two, even if it resembles it in specific nature. And the negated [composition] when one of the three is absent is the second composition subsisting in the three, not the first composition subsisting in the two. So the non-existence of composition between them does not follow. And know that a single individual accident may subsist in a divisible locus such that the accident is divided by its division, so that each part of it exists in a part of its locus; this is uncontroversial. And its subsistence in a divisible locus in such a way that it is not divided by the division of its locus is disputed, as will come. And as for its subsistence in a locus while the same accident also subsists in another locus, that is what we have mentioned that its falsity is self-evident. And what is transmitted from Abu Hashim concerning composition, if it is interpreted as the first division, then there is no dispute with him except concerning the divisibility of composition and its being entitative. And if it is interpreted as the second division, then after conceding its possibility, the discussion remains about the entitative nature of composition. And the well-known [view] is that his intent is the third division, whose falsity is self-evident.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents the second part of Abu Hashim's view: that composition does not subsist in more than two substances. The argument is that if a single composition subsisted in three parts, then removal of one part would necessitate the nonexistence of the composition (since the locus is gone). But the remaining two parts still have composition, as shown by the remaining difficulty of separation. Therefore, the original composition could not have been the same; hence composition cannot subsist in three parts as one individual. The answer distinguishes between two compositions: one subsisting in two parts and another subsisting in three; they are numerically distinct. So when one part is removed, the composition of the three perishes, but that does not affect the composition of the two. The passage then clarifies three possible meanings of 'an accident subsisting in multiple loci': (1) an accident divided by the division of a single divisible locus (uncontroversial), (2) an accident in a divisible locus without division (disputed), (3) the same individual accident subsisting in two independent loci (self-evidently false). Abu Hashim's claim is usually understood as the third, which is deemed obviously false. This passage thus refines the discussion and shows the correct interpretation.

Key Terms
التأليف
مغاير بالشخص
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that composition in three parts is the same individual as composition in two
Correction: They are numerically different accidents, so removal of one part does not affect the two-part composition.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for a single composition to subsist in three parts?
A) Because the difficulty of separation would increase
B) Because removal of one part would end the composition, yet the remaining two still have a composition, so the first composition must be different
C) Because composition is an accident and cannot be divided
D) Because the free agent only creates compositions in pairs

Key Takeaway
A single composition cannot span more than two substances because the composition of three and two are numerically different accidents.

What does this lesson's distinction between numerically different compositions teach you about the need for precise discernment in your own life? How can you apply this to avoid confusion in your understanding of Allah's attributes?
?

Consider your intentions and actions: are they the same 'composition' as before, or have they become something new? Three Ways an Accident Can Subsist in Multiple Loci
Classification of how an accident can subsist in multiple loci and application to composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible meanings of an accident subsisting in multiple loci and which one applies to Abu Hashim's view on composition.

Core Idea
Only the third meaning—same accident in two independent loci—is self-evidently false.

After addressing the objection, the author clarifies the different ways an individual accident can relate to multiple loci. This distinction helps interpret Abu Hashim's claim correctly.

Guided Path
1
Learn the three divisions
Read about each division: (1) accident divided by division of a single locus, (2) accident in a divisible locus without dividing, (3) same accident in two independent loci.
The first is uncontroversial, the second is disputed, the third is self-evidently false.
2
Identify Abu Hashim's intent
According to the well-known view, Abu Hashim meant the third division.
This is why his claim is considered false.
3
See the self-evident falsity
Why can't the same individual accident subsist in two separate loci? Because it would require the accident to be in two places at once without division.
This is impossible by first principles.

Source Text

Introduction to the Second Observatory: The Generality of Quantity
The author introduces the second observatory on quantity and explains its precedence over quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the category of quantity is given precedence over quality and relative accidents, and how its divisions make it more universal and firmly existent.

Core Idea
Quantity is more general in existence than quality and more firmly established than relative accidents.

In the second observatory of al-Mawaqif, the author turns to the category of quantity (al-kamm). He explains that he has given it precedence over quality and other categories because it is more general in existence—one of its divisions, continuous and discrete quantities, is found more widely than qualities, and it is more firmly existent than relative accidents. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice that the author states two reasons for precedence: generality and firmness of existence.
The phrase 'أصح وجوداً' means 'more firmly existent', indicating stability.
2
Compare quantity with quality
Understand why quantity is more general than quality. Quality's kinds are limited: some qualities only exist in certain beings (e.g., psychological qualities only in souls). Quantity, however, applies to both physical and abstract entities without mediation.
Consider the commentary's clarification about abstract entities.
3
Understand the stability of quantity
Reflect on the claim that quantity is 'more firmly existent' than relative accidents. Relative accidents like 'where' (al-ayn) lack stability in subjects, while quantities and qualities are more stable.
The author uses 'asahh wujudan' to mean 'more established in existence'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني في الكم] قدمه على سائر المقولات لكونه أعم وجوداً من الكيف فإنَّ أحد قسميه أعني المقارنات والمجردات وأصح وجوداً من الأعراض النسبية التي لا تقرر لها في ذوات موضوعاتها كتقرر الكميات والكيفيات ) وفيه مقاصد ) تسعة . قوله : ( يعم المقارنات إلخ) أي جميعاً ولذا أورد صيغة الجمع بخلاف الكيف فإن أنواعه الثلاثة أعني الكيفيات المحسوسة والكيفيات الاستعدادية والكيفيات المختصة بالكميات لا توجد في المجردات بلا واسطة والكيفيات النفسانية لا توجد في البسائط العنصرية والجماد لكونها مختصة بذوات الأنفس كما سيجيء وإنما قلنا بلا واسطة لوجود الكيفيات المختصة : بالكميات في المجردات بواسطة العدد كالزوجية والفردية وما قيل إنّ الكيفيات النفسانية لا توجد في المجردات لأن علومها حضورية فمع عدم مساعدة الدليل الذي أقاموا على إثبات العلم لها مخالف لما سيجيء في كلام الشارح قدس سره، حيث فسر الكيفيات النفسانية بالكيفيات المختصة بذوات الأنفس من الأجسام العنصرية ثم قال : ومعنى الاختصاص لها أن تلك الكيفيات توجد في الحيوان دون النباتات والجماد ؛ فعلى هذا لا يتجه أن بعض هذه الكيفيات كالحياة والقدرة والعلم والإرادة ثابتة للواجب والمجردات فلا تكون مختصة بالحيوانات انتهى . قوله: (وأصح وجوداً أي أثبت وجوداً في موضوعه من الصحة بمعنى الثبوت كما يدل عليه البيان فلا يرد ما قيل : إِنَّه لا نزاع في وجود الأين والمتكلمون ينكرون الكم مطلقاً فما معنى أصحية وجوده بالنسبة إلى الأين.
[The second observatory on quantity] He introduced it before the other categories because it is more general in existence than quality, for one of its two divisions, namely the concording and the abstract, and more firmly existent than the relative accidents which have no stability in the substances of their subjects like the stability of quantities and qualities. And it has nine sections. His saying: '(It comprises the concording etc.)' i.e. all of them, therefore he used the plural form, unlike quality, for its three kinds—namely sensible qualities, dispositional qualities, and qualities specific to quantities—are not found in abstract entities without mediation, while psychological qualities are not found in elemental simples and inanimate bodies because they are specific to beings with souls, as will come. And we said 'without mediation' because of the existence of qualities specific to quantities in abstract entities via number such as evenness and oddness. And what was said that psychological qualities do not exist in abstract entities because their knowledges are presential—so with the lack of support from the proof they established for affirming knowledge for them, it contradicts what will come in the words of the commentator, may his secret be sanctified, where he interpreted psychological qualities as qualities specific to beings with souls from elemental bodies, then said: And the meaning of specificity for them is that those qualities exist in animals, not plants and inanimate bodies. So on this, it is not valid that some of these qualities like life, power, knowledge, and will are established for the Necessary and abstract entities, so they would not be specific to animals. End. His saying: 'And more firmly existent' i.e. more firmly established in its subject from 'correctness' (al-ṣiḥḥa) in the sense of stability (al-thubūt), as indicated by the explanation. So it is not refuted by what was said: 'There is no dispute about the existence of the where (al-ayn), and the mutakallimūn deny quantity absolutely, so what is the meaning of its greater firmness of existence compared to the where?'
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to the second observatory of al-Mawaqif, which deals with quantity (al-kamm). The author states that he gave quantity precedence over the other categories because it is more general in existence than quality. He explains that one division of quantity—namely, continuous quantity (al-muqārināt) and discrete quantity (al-mujarradāt)—is more firmly existent than relative accidents, which lack stability in their subjects. The commentator then expands on the generality of quantity by comparing it to quality: the three types of quality are not found in abstract entities without mediation, while psychological qualities are limited to living beings. He also clarifies the remark about 'more firmly existent' by interpreting it as 'more firmly established' (athbat wujūdan), rejecting an objection that the mutakallimūn deny quantity altogether.

Key Terms
الكم (al-kamm)
الكيف (al-kayf)
المقارنات (al-muqārināt)
المجردات (al-mujarradāt)
الأعراض النسبية (al-aʿrāḍ al-nisbiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quantity only applies to material objects.
Correction: Quantity applies to both continuous (material) and discrete (abstract) entities, making it more general in existence than quality.

Checkpoint
Why does the author give quantity precedence over quality?
A) Because quantity is more specific in existence.
B) Because quantity is more general in existence and more firmly existent than relative accidents.
C) Because quality is not a real category.
D) Because quantity is the only category that applies to abstract entities.

Key Takeaway
Quantity is given precedence because it is more general in existence than quality and more firmly existent than relative accidents.

The lesson describes one division of quantity as 'more firmly existent' than relative accidents. Consider the 'relative accidents' in your life—relationships, status, or transient feelings. Are you building your spiritual foundation on something as stable and general as quantity (e.g., consistent acts of worship) or on shifting relative things? The Two Divisions of Quantity: Conceptual and Actual
Commentary on divisibility, defining conceptual and actual division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how quantity is known through its necessary concomitants and the difference between conceptual and actual division.

Core Idea
Quantity is known through its concomitants, not its essence; division is either conceptual or actual.

We begin by examining how we can understand quantity at all. Since genera cannot be known directly, we must rely on their inseparable properties. For quantity, the first such property is divisibility. The commentator clarifies that divisibility can be understood in two distinct ways: in the mind and in external reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the commentator uses 'قوله' to quote and then explain.
This passage is a commentary on a text; the phrases in parentheses are quotes from the original.
2
Identify the means of knowing quantity
The passage begins by stating that quantity is known through its necessary concomitants (lawāzim), not by grasping its essence. Understand why this is important for defining categories.
The conceptual division (wahmiyya) is introduced immediately after.
3
Distinguish the two divisions
Find where conceptual and actual division are defined. Conceptual division applies to all quantities, while actual division applies only to discrete quantities. Note that continuous quantity does not accept actual division because it destroys the original magnitude.
The definition of actual division includes the condition that the original magnitude ceases to exist.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this distinction matters. It affects how we talk about the infinite divisibility of matter and the nature of continuous quantities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( يتوصل إلخ ) أي يكون مرآة لمعرفة حقيقته ولو بوجه ما فإن الأجناس لا يمكن معرفتها إلا باللوازم لا أنه يتوصل إلى كنه حقيقته . قوله : ( شامل إلخ ) فإنّ وجود الأقسام لا ينافي الفرض بل هو أهون عليه . قوله : ( بواسطة اقتران إلخ) يعني أنها واسطة في العروض . قوله : (حقيقة) أشار إلى أنه قد يطلق القابل على ما لا يجتمع مع المقبول مجازاً. قوله : ( في حد ذاته لا باعتبار الالتئام والتركيب . قوله : ( وإلا كان إلخ) أي وأن لا يكون زوال مقدار وحدوث مقدارین ،آخرین بل يكونان موجودين فيه بالفعل مع بقاء المقدار الأول بعينه بقاء المقدار الأول بعينه، فيكون قابلاً للقسمة الفعلية لذاته وقد ثبت أنه قابل لانقسامات غير متناهية بناء على امتناع الجزء يلزم وجود متصلات غير متناهية بالفعل في مقدار واحد فلا يرد أن اللازم مما ذكر أن يكون في المتصل الواحد متصلات متناهية حسب قوله : ( وأصح وجوداً إلخ) فيه تأمل إذ لا نزاع في وجود الأين على أن المتكلمين ينكرون الكم مطلقاً فما معنى أصحية وجوده بالنسبة إلى الأين قوله : (سواء كان بالقطع أو بالكسر لعل حصر سبب الفك في القطع والكسر كما هو المفهوم الظاهر من كلماتهم بحسب الغالب، وإلا فقد يكون الفك بدونهما كما إذا جزئ خيط من طرفيه فانفك بعض أجزائه عن بعض، إذ ليس هذا الفك بطريق الكسر وهو ظاهر ولا بطريق القطع لاحتياجه إلى آلة نفاذة كما صرح به في موقف الجوهر. قوله : ( غير متناهية بحسب الانقسامات الممكنة فيه نظر لأن الانقسام الخارجي يصل إلى حد يقف عنده فلا يلزم الاشتمال على مقادير غير متناهية بالفعل.
His saying: '(One arrives etc.)' i.e. it is a mirror for knowing its reality, even if in some respect, for genera cannot be known except through their concomitants, not that one arrives at the essence of its reality. His saying: '(Including etc.)' i.e. the existence of divisions does not contradict the supposition; rather it is easier for it. His saying: '(By the accompaniment of etc.)' means it is a medium in attribution. His saying: '(Reality)' indicated that the receptive may be applied metaphorically to what does not combine with the received. His saying: '(In itself, not by consideration of composition and structure)'. His saying: '(Otherwise it would be etc.)' i.e. that there would not be the disappearance of one magnitude and the occurrence of two other magnitudes, but rather they would exist in it actually while the first magnitude remains exactly as it was, so it would be receptive to actual division in itself, and it has been established that it is receptive to infinite divisions based on the impossibility of the part, necessitating the existence of actually infinite continuous magnitudes in one magnitude. So it is not refuted that what is necessary from what was mentioned is that in one continuous magnitude there are finite continuous magnitudes? according to his saying: '(And more firmly existent etc.)' in which is consideration, for there is no dispute about the existence of the where, given that the mutakallimūn deny quantity absolutely, so what is the meaning of its greater firmness of existence compared to the where? His saying: '(Whether by cutting or by breaking)' perhaps the restriction of the cause of separation to cutting and breaking is as understood apparently from their words according to the usual, otherwise separation may occur without them, as when a thread is pulled from its two ends and some of its parts separate from others, for this separation is not by way of breaking—that is obvious—nor by way of cutting, because it needs a penetrating tool, as declared in the position of the atom. His saying: '(Infinite according to possible divisions') in which is a consideration, because external division reaches a limit at which it stops, so it does not necessitate including actually infinite magnitudes.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is the commentator's explanation of the first property of quantity: that it accepts division (al-qisma). He begins by clarifying that the means to know the reality of quantity is through its necessary concomitants (lawāzim), not by grasping its essence directly. Then he explains that the two types of division—conceptual (wahmiyya) and actual (fiʿliyya)—both apply: the existence of actual divisions does not prevent conceptual division. He then discusses how quantity is a medium in the attribution of divisibility to substances. The commentator also addresses the objection that if a magnitude after division retained its original identity, it would imply infinite actual divisions, but he responds that actual division destroys the original magnitude and produces two new ones. Finally, he comments on the phrase 'more firmly existent' in the introduction, noting a tension because the mutakallimūn deny quantity, and he also examines the usual restriction of actual division to cutting or breaking.

Key Terms
القسمة (al-qisma): division
الوهمية (al-wahmiyya): conceptual
الفعلية (al-fiʿliyya): actual
الكم المتصل (al-kamm al-muttaṣil): continuous quantity
اللازم (al-lāzim): necessary concomitant

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that actual division applies to continuous quantity (like a line) without changing its nature.
Correction: The commentator explains that actual division destroys the original magnitude; a line divided into two parts is no longer the same line.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, how can quantity be known?
A) Through its essence directly.
B) Through its necessary concomitants.
C) Through intuitive knowledge.
D) Through empirical observation alone.

Key Takeaway
The first property of quantity is divisibility, which can be conceptual (mental) or actual (physical), and only conceptual division applies to continuous quantity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the gulf between your knowledge of Allah's signs and His essence, does it humble you or frustrate you? How will you respond to the reality that you can only know Him through His concomitants, not directly? Commit today to contemplating His creation more deeply as the mirror of His attributes.
?
In your life, do you tend to 'conceptually' divide your faith from your daily actions, keeping your beliefs intact while your deeds contradict them? Or do your actions actually divide and destroy the integrity of your faith? Examine one area where your practice does not match your profession and resolve to make it one.
?
The passage teaches that actual division destroys the original magnitude. Think of a habit or commitment you have repeatedly broken: does each breach destroy the original intention, leaving you with a fragmented self? How can you restore the unity of purpose that conforms to true tawhid? Write down one specific step to rebuild it.

Objections and Clarifications on Actual Division
Commentary addressing objections about infinite actual divisions and the means of actual division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that infinite actual divisions would be required, and the commentator's response regarding the restriction of actual division to cutting or breaking.

Core Idea
Actual division does not lead to infinite actual magnitudes; it stops at a limit.

After defining actual division, the commentator addresses a potential objection: if a continuous quantity is actually divisible infinitely, wouldn't that imply infinitely many actual parts within a finite magnitude? He then discusses how actual division is performed and why the mutakallimūn deny quantity itself, and finally restricts the means of actual division to cutting or breaking in most cases.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Notice that it continues from the previous lesson and addresses the phrase 'more firmly existent' and the restriction to cutting or breaking.
The passage includes a critique of the phrase 'more firmly existent' in relation to the mutakallimūn's denial of quantity.
2
Understand the objection
The commentator raises a point: if actual division could be repeated infinitely, it would necessitate infinitely many actual continuous magnitudes within one. The passage argues that external division reaches a limit.
This touches on the debate about infinite divisibility and actual infinites.
3
Analyze the restriction to cutting or breaking
The commentator notes that the usual way to effect actual division is by cutting or breaking, but there are exceptions (e.g., pulling a thread from both ends). Understand why this distinction matters.
The restriction is about the cause of separation, not the definition of division.

?

How does recognizing your finite capacity drive you to rely more on Allah's infinite mercy?

The Second Property: Continuous Quantity Does Not Accept Actual Division
The author explains why continuous quantity does not accept actual division and responds to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why continuous quantity cannot be actually divided while remaining the same, and how the mutakallimūn's objection is addressed.

Core Idea
When a body is split, its original continuous quantity vanishes and two new quantities appear.

The author continues his analysis of quantity by examining the second property: that continuous magnitude does not allow actual division while staying the same. He argues that if the receiver (the body) remained with the received (the quantity) after division, it wouldn't be truly receptive. This leads to the conclusion that the original quantity disappears and two new ones emerge, addressing a potential objection from those who deny quantity altogether.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
Read the passage and note the central claim: continuous quantity does not accept actual division.
The term 'actual division' (al-qisma al-fiʿliyya) refers to physical separation.
2
Understand the argument
The argument rests on two points: (1) the receiver remains with the received, otherwise it wouldn't be receptive; (2) upon separation, the original quantity does not remain because it is one continuous whole without division. Instead, two new quantities appear.
If the original quantity remained, infinite quantities would coexist, which is absurd.
3
Consider the objection and response
The author addresses the mutakallimūn who deny quantity entirely. He asserts that continuous quantity inherent in matter characterizes it, and this explanation handles the objection about the 'where' (al-ayn).
This shows the integration of philosophical argument with theological concerns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) المعنى ( الثاني لا يقبله الكم المتصل الذي هو المقدار ( فإن القابل يبقى مع المقبول ) وإلا لم يكن قابلاً له حقيقة بالضرورة ) وعند الفك ) والفصل الوارد على الجسم ( لا يبقى الكم ) أي : المقدار ( الأول بعينه ) ؛ لأنه متصل واحد في حد ذاته لا مفصل فيه أصلاً ) بل يزول ويحصل ) هناك ( كمان ) أي : مقداران (آخران ) لم يكونا موجودين بالفعل وإلا كان في متصل واحد متصلات غير متناهية بحسب الانقسامات الممكنة ( نعم الكم المتصل الحال في المادة الجسمية : ( يعد المادة عليه البيان فلا يرد ما قيل : إِنَّه لا نزاع في وجود الأين والمتكلمون ينكرون الكم مطلقاً فما معنى أصحية وجوده بالنسبة إلى الأين.
And the second meaning—continuous quantity, which is magnitude, does not accept it; for the receiver remains with the received; otherwise it would not be receptive to it necessarily in reality. And upon separation and division occurring to the body, the quantity (i.e. the first magnitude) does not remain exactly as it was, because it is one continuous [magnitude] in itself, having no division in it at all; rather it disappears and there occur there two quantities (i.e. two other magnitudes) that were not actually existent; otherwise there would be in one continuous [magnitude] infinite continuous [magnitudes] according to possible divisions. Yes, the continuous quantity inherent in corporeal matter characterizes the matter. Upon it is the explanation, so it is not refuted by what was said: 'There is no dispute about the existence of the where, and the mutakallimūn deny quantity absolutely, so what is the meaning of its greater firmness of existence compared to the where?'
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the second property of quantity: that continuous quantity (al-kamm al-muttaṣil), i.e. magnitude (al-miqdār), does not accept actual division. The author argues that if a receiver (the body) remains with the received (the quantity) after division, then it would not be truly receptive. When a body is separated, the original continuous quantity does not persist—it disappears and two new magnitudes appear that were not previously actual. If they were actual, then infinite continuous magnitudes would coexist, which is impossible. The commentator adds that continuous quantity inherent in matter characterizes the matter, and this explanation avoids the objection about the where (al-ayn) and the mutakallimūn's denial of quantity.

Key Terms
الكم المتصل (al-kamm al-muttaṣil): continuous quantity
المقدار (al-miqdār): magnitude
القسمة الفعلية (al-qisma al-fiʿliyya): actual division
الفك (al-fakk): separation
الكم (al-kamm): quantity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that breaking a body preserves the same quantity, only divided into parts.
Correction: The original continuous quantity disappears upon division; two new quantities of different identity come into existence.

Checkpoint
What happens to the original continuous quantity when a body is separated?
A) It remains exactly as it was.
B) It disappears and two new magnitudes appear.
C) It splits into two halves of the same magnitude.
D) It becomes a discrete quantity.

Key Takeaway
Continuous quantity does not persist through actual division; division creates two new quantities that were not previously actual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If continuous quantity cannot be actually divided, what does that imply about the physical objects you interact with every day? Are you too confident in your perception of them as composed of separable parts? How does this truth humble you before the One who knows the true reality of things?

The author argues that the original magnitude vanishes and two new ones appear upon division. Does this not make you reflect on the transient nature of the material world—how even the 'same' object, when changed, becomes something entirely new in a deep sense? How should this affect your attachment to worldly possessions?
?

Yet even quantity is not permanent; it disappears and reappears with division. What does this teach you about relying on anything other than the Eternal? Three Meanings of Fakk and Their Application to Discrete Quantity
Al-Jurjani distinguishes three meanings of separative division and shows which apply to discrete quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct meanings of separative division (al-qisma al-infikakiyya) and which of them apply to discrete quantity as opposed to continuous quantity.

Core Idea
Only essential separation applies to the units themselves; real connection and contiguity do not.

In this passage, al-Jurjani builds on earlier discussions of divisibility by examining the specific case of discrete quantity. He argues that the substrate for separative division is matter, not magnitude, and then carefully distinguishes three senses of 'splitting' — only one of which actually applies to the units of discrete quantity. Understanding this distinction clarifies why discrete quantities cannot be divided in the same way as continuous ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the substrate for separative division
Read the first sentence: 'فالقابل للقسمة الانفكاكية هو المادة الباقية بعينها مع الانفصال دون المقدار الذي هو الكم المتصل.' Note that the author explicitly states that the substrate is matter, not magnitude (continuous quantity). Ask yourself why matter is the proper substrate.
This sets the stage for the three meanings.
2
Understand the first meaning: removal of real connection
Read the second sentence: 'إذا أريد بها زوال الاتصال الحقيقي...' Al-Jurjani says this meaning applies to neither continuous nor discrete quantity. For discrete quantity, the units are already separate, so there is no real connection to remove.
This meaning is impossible for both types of quantity.
3
Examine the second meaning: removal of contiguity
Read the third sentence: 'وإذا أريد بها زوال الاتصال بحسب المجاورة...' This meaning applies to the substrate of units (the matter that holds them together) but not to the units themselves. Contiguity is a relation between separate things, not intrinsic to the units.
4
Analyze the third meaning: essential separation
Read the fourth sentence: 'وإذا أريد بها عدم الاتصال مطلقاً أعني الانفصال الذاتي...' This meaning applies to the units themselves, because they are by nature discrete. This is the only meaning that actually characterizes discrete quantity.
This is the key takeaway: discrete quantity is essentially separable.
5
Synthesize the three meanings
Review all three meanings together. Notice how each targets a different aspect: real connection (nonexistent), contiguity (relational), and essential separation (intrinsic). This shows that discrete quantity is not 'splittable' in the same way as a continuous line.
This distinction is crucial for later discussions in kalam and physics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالقابل للقسمة الانفكاكية هو المادة الباقية بعينها مع الانفصال دون المقدار الذي هو الكم المتصل. ثم نقول: إن القسمة الفكية إذا أريد بها زوال الاتصال الحقيقي فهي كما لا تعرض للكم المتصل لا تعرض للكم المنفصل أيضاً لأن معروض الوحدات من حيث أنه معروض لها لا يكون متصلاً واحداً في نفسه بل منفصلا بعضه عن بعض فلا يتصور هناك زوال اتصال حقيقي. وإذا أريد بها زوال الاتصال بحسب المجاورة كانت عارضة لمعروض الوحدات بالذات لا للواحدات في أنفسها. وإذا أريد بها عدم الاتصال مطلقاً أعني الانفصال الذاتي فهي عارضة للوحدات.
So the substrate for separative division is matter remaining itself with separation, not the magnitude which is continuous quantity. Then we say: if by fakk division is meant the removal of real connection, then just as it does not occur to continuous quantity, so it does not occur to discrete quantity either, because the substrate of units, inasmuch as it is a substrate for them, is not a single continuous thing in itself but rather separated from each other, so there is no removal of real connection conceivable. And if by it is meant the removal of connection in terms of contiguity, then it occurs to the substrate of units in itself, not to the units themselves. And if by it is meant absolute non-connection, i.e., essential separation, then it occurs to the units.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage discusses the substrate for separative division (al-qisma al-infikakiyya). The author states that the true substrate for such division is matter, not the magnitude (continuous quantity). He then analyzes three meanings of 'fakk division' (splitting). First meaning: removal of real connection (zawal al-ittisal al-haqiqi). This does not occur to continuous quantity (as previously shown) nor to discrete quantity, because the substrate of units (the units themselves are discrete) is not a single continuous thing—it is already separated—so no real connection is removed. Second meaning: removal of connection in terms of contiguity (zawal al-ittisal bihasab al-mujawara). This occurs to the substrate of units (i.e., the matter that holds them together) but not to the units themselves. Third meaning: absolute non-connection, i.e., essential separation (al-infisal al-dhati). This occurs to the units themselves, as they are by nature discrete. Thus, discrete quantity is only subject to the third meaning, not the first two.

Key Terms
القسمة الانفكاكية
الاتصال الحقيقي
المجاورة
الانفصال الذاتي
معروض الوحدات
الوحدات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that discrete quantity can be split in the same sense as continuous quantity, i.e., by removing a real connection.
Correction: Discrete quantity's units are already separate, so only essential separation (the third meaning) applies to them. The first two meanings are either impossible or apply only to the substrate.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which meaning of fakk division applies to the units (al-wahadat) themselves?
A) Removal of real connection.
B) Removal of connection by contiguity.
C) Absolute non-connection or essential separation.
D) Both A and B.

Key Takeaway
Discrete quantity is only subject to fakk in the sense of essential separation, not real connection or contiguity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own beliefs, do you treat them as a continuous whole that cannot be broken apart, or as separate units you can examine individually? How might confusing these types of separation lead to error in your understanding?
?
This lesson shows that 'essential separation' is a recognition of how things truly are, not a change. In your spiritual life, are there areas where you need to recognize an inherent separation—such as between your actions and your true self—rather than forcing a false unity?

The First Property of Quantity: Divisibility
Commentator distinguishes three meanings of divisive division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three meanings of divisive division (al-qisma al-infikākiyya) and how they apply to continuous and discrete quantity.

Core Idea
Divisibility of quantity is not a single concept but has three distinct meanings.

The commentator begins by examining the property of divisibility specific to quantity. He distinguishes three interpretations of 'divisive division' to clarify which meaning truly belongs to quantity as such.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on divisibility
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully, noting the three interpretations introduced by 'if by it is meant'.
Pay attention to the technical terms: ittiṣāl ḥaqīqī, mujāwara, infiṣāl dhātī.
2
Identify the three meanings
List the three ways 'divisive division' is understood: removal of real continuity, removal of adjacency, and absolute separation.
Each meaning applies to different aspects of quantity.
3
Analyze the application
For each meaning, determine whether it applies to continuous quantity (al-kamm al-muttaṣil) or discrete quantity (al-kamm al-munfaṣil), and to the units themselves or their subject.
The subject of units is the underlying substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : إن القسمة الفكية إذا أريد بها زوال الاتصال الحقيقي فهي كما لا تعرض للكم المتصل لا تعرض للكم المنفصل أيضاً لأن معروض الوحدات من حيث أنه معروض لها لا يكون متصلاً واحداً في نفسه بل منفصلا بعضه عن بعض فلا يتصور هناك زوال اتصال حقيقي وإذا أريد بها زوال الاتصال بحسب المجاورة كانت عارضة لمعروض الوحدات بالذات لا للواحدات في أنفسها وإذا أريد بها عدم الاتصال مطلقاً أعني الانفصال الذاتي فهي عارضة للوحدات بالذات فإنها في ذواتها منفصلة بعضها عن بعض وعارضة لمعروضات الوحدات بواسطتها الخاصة الثانية وجود عاد فيه يعده إما بالفعل كما في العدد فإن كل عدد يوجد فيه الواحد بالفعل وهو عاد له وقد يعد بعض الأعداد بعضها أيضاً وإما بالقوة كما في المقدار فإن كل مقدار خطاً كان أو سطحاً أو جسماً يمكن أن يفرض فيه واحد يعده كما يعد الأشل وهو حبل طوله ستون ذراعاً بالأذرع أنك العد إذا أسقطت منه أمثاله أي من المعدود أمثال العاد فني المعدود وقد يفسر العد باستيعاب العاد للمعدود بالتطبيق لكنه مخصوص بالمقادير ولا يتناول العدد إذ لا معنى لتطبيق الوحدة على الوحدة الخاصة الثالثة المساواة ومقابلاها أعني الزيادة والنقصان فإن العقل إذا لاحظ المقادير أو الأعداد ولم يلاحظ معها شيئاً آخراً أمكنه الحكم بينها بالمساواة أو الزيادة أو النقصان وإذا لاحظ شيئاً آخراً ولم يلاحظ معه عدداً ولا مقداراً لم يمكنه الحكم بشيء منها فقبول هذه الأمور من خواص الكم
Then we say: Indeed, the divisive division – if by it is meant the removal of real continuity, then it is just as it does not occur to continuous quantity, it also does not occur to discrete quantity, because the subject of the units, inasmuch as it is a subject for them, is not a continuous single thing in itself, but rather is separate, some from some, so there is no conception of removal of real continuity there. And if by it is meant removal of continuity in respect of adjacency, then it occurs to the subject of the units essentially, not to the units in themselves. And if by it is meant absence of continuity absolutely, i.e., essential separation, then it occurs to the units essentially, for they are in their essences separate, some from some, and it occurs to the subjects of the units by their special mediation. The second: existence of a divisor in it that counts it, either in actuality as in number, for every number has the one in actuality and it is a divisor of it, and some numbers also count others; or potentially as in magnitude, for every magnitude – be it line, surface, or body – it is possible to assume in it a one that counts it, just as the ashl (a rope of sixty cubits) is counted by cubits, that you count when you remove from it its likes, i.e., from the counted, the likes of the counter, so the counted perishes. And counting has been interpreted as the counter encompassing the counted by application, but it is specific to magnitudes and does not include numbers, for there is no meaning to applying the unit to the particular unit. The third: equality and its opposites, namely increase and decrease. For the intellect, when it observes magnitudes or numbers and does not observe with them anything else, can judge among them with equality, increase, or decrease. And when it observes something else and does not observe with it a number or a magnitude, it cannot judge with any of them. So accepting these things is among the properties of quantity.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a classification by the commentator of the three essential properties specific to quantity (al-kamm). The commentator first discusses the property of divisive division (al-qisma al-infikākiyya). He explains that if by this division we mean the removal of real continuity (zawāl al-ittiṣāl al-ḥaqīqī), then it does not apply to either continuous or discrete quantity because the subject of the units (ma‘rūḍ al-waḥdāt) is inherently separate. If instead we mean removal of continuity in terms of adjacency (bi-ḥasab al-mujāwara), it applies to the subject of the units essentially but not to the units themselves. If we mean absolute absence of continuity, i.e., essential separation (al-infiṣāl al-dhātī), then it applies to the units themselves, which are essentially separate. The commentator then enumerates the second property: having a divisor (al-‘ād). Number has a divisor in actuality (bi-al-fi‘l) because the one is found in every number actually. Some numbers count others as well. Magnitude has a divisor potentially (bi-al-quwwa); one can assume a unit in any magnitude. The example of the ashl (a sixty-cubit rope) counted by cubits illustrates how the counter is removed from the counted until the counted is exhausted. He notes that interpreting counting as application (taṭbīq) is restricted to magnitudes and does not apply to numbers because units cannot be applied to each other. The third property is equality (al-musāwāh) and its opposites: increase (al-ziyāda) and decrease (al-nuqṣān). The intellect, when considering magnitudes or numbers without any other factor, can judge them as equal, greater, or lesser. If another factor is introduced, such judgment is not possible. Thus, these three features—divisibility, having a divisor, and equality/inequality—are essential properties of quantity.

Key Terms
قسمة انفكاكية
اتصال حقيقي
مجاورة
انفصال ذاتي
كم متصل
كم منفصل
معروض الوحدات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which meaning of divisive division applies to the units themselves?
A) Removal of real continuity
B) Removal of adjacency
C) Essential separation
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Divisibility of quantity is understood in three ways, each with a different subject of attribution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own beliefs—about God, about yourself—how often do you assume that a term like 'oneness' or 'existence' has only one simple meaning? Are you willing to let this lesson's method of precise distinction challenge your assumptions, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
The commentator shows that even a concept like 'division' has layers. In your own life, where have you oversimplified a matter—thinking you understood it fully—only to later realize there were hidden distinctions you missed? Will you commit to seeking that deeper clarity, especially in matters of faith?
?
Just as discrete quantity's units are essentially separate, yet can be considered together, your actions and intentions may be separate yet form a whole before Allah. Are you aware of the 'units' of your life (e.g., your prayers, your words) and how they are related? Do they have a real connection, or merely adjacency? What would it mean to seek real continuity in your relationship with Him?

The Second Property of Quantity: Having a Divisor
Commentator discusses the second property: existence of a divisor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the property of 'having a divisor' in quantity, its division into actual and potential, and the difference between number and magnitude in this regard.

Core Idea
Every quantity has a divisor that measures it, either actually in numbers or potentially in magnitudes.

The commentator now turns to the second essential property of quantity: the existence of a divisor (al-`ād). He explains that this property is found in both number and magnitude, but in different modes: actual for numbers, potential for magnitudes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the divisor
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the distinction between actual (bi al-fi‘l) and potential (bi al-quwwa) existence of a divisor.
Look for the example of the ashl (rope) to understand potential divisor.
2
Understand the two modes
Identify how the divisor exists in number (actual) and in magnitude (potential).
In number, the one is actually found; in magnitude, a unit can be assumed.
3
Consider the interpretation of counting
Note the commentator's remark about the interpretation of counting as application (taṭbīq) and why it is specific to magnitudes.
Why can't numbers be counted by application?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The divisor in magnitudes is only potential, not actual. Will you expand your expectations today?
?

But consider your heart's devotion: is it an actual 'number' you can tally (five prayers, one charity) or a potential 'magnitude' that should grow without limit? Which type describes your faith, and what change does this distinction demand of you?

The Third Property of Quantity: Equality and Its Opposites
Commentator identifies equality and its opposites as properties of quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that equality, increase, and decrease are properties specific to quantity when considered in itself, without relation to other things.

Core Idea
Quantity alone, when isolated, can be judged as equal, greater, or lesser; no other category allows such judgment.

The commentator concludes the properties of quantity with equality (musāwāh) and its opposites, increase and decrease. He explains that the intellect can only make these judgments when considering quantities alone, without any extrinsic factor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on equality
Read the Arabic and translation, noticing the condition placed on the intellect's judgment: it must observe only magnitudes or numbers, nothing else.
Contrast this with when another thing is observed.
2
Understand the exclusivity
Grasp why these judgments are exclusive to quantity: when something else is observed, e.g., quality or relation, such judgments are not possible.
Example: two red objects cannot be compared in redness as 'equal' without quantifying redness.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why equality/inequality being properties of quantity is important for kalam, especially in arguments about divine attributes.
Thinking about quantitative language applied to Allah.

The Second Property: Existence of a Divisor
Introduces the second property with actual/potential distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second property of quantity — the existence of a divisor — and how it applies differently to numbers (actual) and magnitudes (potential).

Core Idea
Every quantity has a divisor, but in numbers it is actual; in magnitudes it is potential.

The second property of quantity concerns the presence of a divisor within the quantity. The author distinguishes between numbers, where the unit is an actual divisor, and magnitudes, where a unit can be assumed as a divisor. This lesson presents the core definition and the counting process through subtraction of equals.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the definition of the second property and the distinction between actual and potential division.
Focus on the example of the 60-cubit rope.
2
Identify the two types of division
Notice the explicit contrast between 'bi-l-fi'l' (actual) for numbers and 'bi-l-quwwa' (potential) for magnitudes. Think about why numbers have division actually present.
The unit is always present in a number.
3
Understand the counting process
The passage explains 'counting' as subtracting equals until exhaustion. This is a key part of the definition. Consider how this applies to both numbers and magnitudes.
4
Self-check
Pause and answer: In what sense is a number ‘countable’ by its units? How does this differ from dividing a line into cubits?
Use the quiz to reinforce.

الثانية وجود عاد فيه يعده إما بالفعل كما في العدد فإن كل عدد يوجد فيه الواحد بالفعل وهو عاد له، وقد يعد بعض الأعداد بعضها أيضاً و إما كما في المقدار فإن كل مقدار خطاً كان أو سطحاً أو جسماً يمكن أن يفرض فيه واحد يعده كما يعد الأشل وهو حبل طوله ستون ذراعاً بالأذرع أنك العد إذا أسقطت منه أمثاله أي من المعدود أمثال العاد فني المعدود وقد يفسر العد باستيعاب العاد للمعدود بالتطبيق لكنه مخصوص بالمقادير ولايتناول العدد إذ لا معنى لتطبيق الوحدة على الوحدة الخاصة. ومعنى قوله: (مخصوص بالمقادير ولا يتناول العدد إلخ) يعني : إن المقادير لما كانت متفاوتة بالزيادة والنقصان يمكن التطبيق بينها بخلاف العدد فإنه مركب من الوحدات والوحدات لا يتصور فيها التفاوت بالزيادة والنقصان فلا معنى للتطبيق فيها وما قيل : إن التطبيق جعل العاد مطابقاً للمعدود والمطابقة هي الاتحاد في الأطراف، ولا أطراف للوحدات فلا يخفى ضعفه. قوله : ( الثانية وجود عاد إلخ) اعترض عليه العلامة الشيرازي بأن المقدار الأصم لا يوجد فيه العاد لأنه مفسر بما لا عاد له وأجاب عنه الشارح في حواشي حكمة العين بأن الأصم قابل للتنصيف قطعاً ونصفه يعده مرتين جزماً ، ولا ينافي ذلك كونه أصم إذ معناه أنه لا يعده المقدار المعين المفروض لتقدير المقادير بمنزلة الواحد في العدد، وعدم عده له لا يستلزم أن لا يعده له مقدار أصلاً. قوله : (إذا أسقطت منه (أمثاله مرات متناهية أو غير متناهية فلا نقض بالعدد الغير المتناهي. قوله: (لكنه مخصوص بالمقادير لأن التطبيق هاهنا بمعنى جعل العاد مطابقاً للمعدود والمطابقة هي الاتحاد في الأطراف كما سبق في المقصد السادس من مرصد الوحدة والكثرة، ولا شك أنها لا تتصور في الوحدات بل في المقادير.
The second (property): existence of a divisor in it, which divides it either actually as in number, for every number has the one actually in it and it is its divisor, and some numbers also divide others; or potentially as in magnitude, for every magnitude, whether line, surface, or body, can have assumed in it a unit that divides it, as the ishl (a rope of sixty cubits) is divided by cubits. That is, you count when you subtract from it its equals, i.e., from the counted things equals of the divisor, until the counted thing becomes exhausted. And counting has been interpreted as the divisor covering the counted thing by application, but this is specific to magnitudes and does not apply to numbers, since there is no meaning to applying the unit onto a specific unit. And the meaning of his statement: 'specific to magnitudes and does not apply to numbers, etc.' means: magnitudes, because they vary in increase and decrease, can be applied to each other, unlike numbers, for number is composed of units, and units are not conceivable to vary in increase and decrease, so there is no meaning to application in them. And what is said: application is making the divisor correspond to the counted thing, and correspondence is unity in the extremities, and there are no extremities for units, so its weakness is not hidden. His statement: 'the second: existence of a divisor, etc.' - The distinguished Shirazi objected to it that the solid (asamm) magnitude does not have a divisor, because it is defined as that which has no divisor. And the commentator responded in his glosses on Hikmat al-Ayn that the solid is certainly divisible in half, and its half divides it twice definitely, and that does not contradict its being solid, for its meaning is that the specific assumed magnitude used for measuring magnitudes like the one in numbers does not divide it, and its not being divided by it does not necessitate that it is not divided by any magnitude at all. His statement: 'if you subtract from it its equals' - whether finitely or infinitely many times, so there is no objection from infinite number. His statement: 'but it is specific to magnitudes' - because application here means making the divisor correspond to the counted thing, and correspondence is unity in the extremities, as preceded in the sixth section of the observation of unity and multiplicity, and there is no doubt that it is not conceivable in units but in magnitudes.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage defines the second property of quantity: the existence of a divisor (al-'ad). It begins by distinguishing two cases: in numbers, the divisor is actual (the unit is present and divides the number), and numbers can also divide each other; in magnitudes, the divisor is potential—one can assume a unit that divides it, as illustrated by the example of a 60-cubit rope being divided by cubits. The counting process is described as subtracting equals until exhaustion. The author then notes an interpretation of counting as 'application' (tatbīq), where the divisor covers the counted thing, but this is specific to magnitudes and not numbers, because units lack extremities for correspondence. The commentary elaborates: magnitudes vary in size, allowing application, while units are uniform and cannot be applied. An objection by al-Shirazi is addressed: the 'solid' magnitude (al-āsamm), defined as having no divisor, is actually divisible in half, so it does have a divisor; 'solid' only means it lacks a specific standard divisor. The discussion of subtracting equals covers both finite and infinite repetitions, avoiding problems with infinite numbers. Finally, the application is confirmed as specific to magnitudes because it requires correspondence of extremities, which only magnitudes have.

Key Terms
العاد
العدد
المقدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The divisor must always be actual.
Correction: In magnitudes, the divisor is only potential; we can assume a unit for counting.

Checkpoint
According to the second property of quantity, what is the difference between numbers and magnitudes regarding the existence of a divisor?
A) Numbers have a potential divisor while magnitudes have an actual divisor.
B) Numbers have an actual divisor (the unit) while magnitudes have a potential divisor that can be assumed.
C) Both numbers and magnitudes have actual divisors.
D) Neither numbers nor magnitudes have divisors.

Key Takeaway
Every quantity has a divisor, but for numbers it is actually present, while for magnitudes we assume a unit to divide it.

6 min
Application (Tatbīq) Is Specific to Magnitudes
Argues that tatbīq interpretation is exclusive to magnitudes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the interpretation of counting as 'application' (tatbīq) applies only to magnitudes and not to numbers.

Core Idea
Application requires matching extremities, which units in numbers lack.

Some scholars interpret counting as 'application' (tatbīq), where the divisor covers the counted thing. However, this interpretation is limited to magnitudes because it requires corresponding extremities, which units do not have. The author explains this with a clear argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on application
The passage first states that tatbīq is specific to magnitudes and does not apply to numbers.
Note the phrase 'makhsūs bi-l-maqādīr'.
2
Understand why magnitudes allow application
Magnitudes vary in size (increase and decrease), so they can be applied to one another. Consider a line segment compared to another.
The example of a rope of 60 cubits is used earlier.
3
Grasp why numbers do not
Numbers are composed of units that are uniform and have no extremities. Applying a unit to another unit is meaningless because they are identical and lack boundaries.
4
Reflect on the argument
The author mentions a weak opinion that application is correspondence in extremities, but units have no extremities, so the view is weak.
This reinforces the limitation.

Objection: The Solid Magnitude (al-Āsamm) and Its Divisor
Resolves an objection about the solid magnitude (al-āsamm) having no divisor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised by al-Shirazi concerning the 'solid' magnitude (al-āsamm) and its resolution: even the solid magnitude is divisible, just not by a standard unit.

Core Idea
The 'solid' magnitude is divisible; 'solid' means it lacks a standard divisor, not any divisor.

An objection arises: if the second property requires a divisor, what about the 'solid' magnitude (al-āsamm) which is defined as having no divisor? The commentator resolves the paradox by clarifying that 'solid' means lacking a standard measuring unit, not complete indivisibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
al-Shirazi objects that a solid magnitude (like sqrt(2)) has no divisor. The commentator answers that it is still divisible in half, so it does have a divisor.
Focus on the definition of 'asamm'.
2
Understand the meaning of 'asamm'
The passage explains that 'asamm' means the magnitude is not divisible by a specific standard unit (like a cubit for lengths), not that it cannot be divided at all.
3
Grasp the logical distinction
Not being divisible by one particular unit does not mean it is indivisible by any unit. Divisions can be made without a standard measure.
4
Note the clarifications about subtraction
The passage then clarifies that subtracting equals can be done finitely or infinitely, so infinite numbers are not a problem.
This connects to the earlier counting process.
