Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A 'solid' magnitude (al-āsamm) has no divisor at all.
Correction: It has no standard divisor (like a common measure), but it is still divisible, e.g., by half.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'solid' (asamm) magnitude mean?
A) It cannot be divided in any way.
B) It cannot be divided by a specific standard measuring unit.
C) It has no divisor because it is infinite.
D) It is only found in geometry.

Key Takeaway
The 'solid' magnitude (al-āsamm) is divisible; it only lacks a standard measuring unit, not a divisor entirely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been absolutely certain that something was impossible—like a problem in your life that seemed to have no solution—only to later discover that you were looking for a specific expected outcome rather than any possible resolution? How does this lesson's clarification about the 'solid' magnitude challenge you to reconsider your own assumptions about what is possible in your spiritual journey? What step can you take today to break through a fixed mindset?
?
The commentator argues that having no divisor from one unit does not mean having no divisor at all. In your relationship with Allah, are there areas where you feel 'solid' (impenetrable) to guidance because you are fixated on your own expectations of how that guidance should come? Ask yourself: What 'standard units' am I insisting must measure my spiritual progress? Am I open to being divided—changed—by unexpected forms of grace?
?
This passage deals with the subtle difference between absolute impossibility and limited impossibility. Think of a personal weakness or sin you have labeled as unchangeable because you haven't found a 'unit' of effort that seems to work. What if you have been seeking a specific method (a conventional divisor) but ignoring other forms of correction? What single action can you take right now that would prove your weakness is actually divisible?

The Third Property of Quantity: Equality, Increase, and Decrease
Defines the third property and shows its dependence on the first property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the intellect judges equality, increase, and decrease in quantities, and how this property is a branch of the first property of quantity.

Core Idea
Equality and inequality are exclusive to quantity and depend on comparing assumed parts.

The text now introduces the third property of quantity, which is the ability to judge equality, increase, or decrease between quantities. This property is closely tied to the first property (acceptance of division) because comparing quantities requires assuming parts. The passage explains this connection and defends it against objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the third property
Carefully read the Arabic lines that define the third property as equality and its counterparts. Notice the condition: the intellect observes magnitudes or numbers alone.
The translation helps clarify the meaning.
2
Identify the essential accident status
Notice that the text says acceptance of these matters is among the properties and essential accidents of quantities. Understand what 'essential accident' means in this context.
It means the property follows the essence of quantity.
3
Understand the branching argument
Read the reasoning that the third property is a branch of the first. The text uses the assumption of parts in two quantities to derive equality or inequality.
This is the core proof connecting the two properties.
4
Reflect on the clarification
The commentary explains that branching does not contradict essential accident status because the first property is a mediating factor in proof, not in attribution.
This resolves a potential confusion.

الثالثة المساواة ومقابلاها أعني الزيادة والنقصان فإن العقل إذا لاحظ المقادير أو الأعداد ولم يلاحظ معها شيئاً آخراً أمكنه الحكم بينها بالمساواة أو الزيادة أو النقصان وإذا لاحظ شيئاً آخراً ولم يلاحظ معه عدداً ولا مقداراً لم يمكنه الحكم بشيء منها فقبول هذه الأمور من خواص الكميات وأعراضها الذاتية. وهو) أي هذا المذكور الذي هو الخاصة الثالثة (فرع الخاصة الأولى لأنه إذا فرض أجزاء في كم فإما أن يوجد بإزاء كل جزء مفروض في ذلك الكم جزء مفروض في كم آخر أو أكثر أو أقل فيتصف حينئذ الكم الأول بالمساواة أو بالنقصان أو بالزيادة مقيساً إلى الكم الثاني. ومنهم من عكس فجعل قبول القسمة فرعاً لقبول المساواة واللامساواة وتوجيهه أن يقال : إن الوهم إنما يقسم المقدار إذا لاحظ مقداراً آخر أصغر منه فيفرض فيه ما يساويه وهو شيء ويبقى الفضل وهو شيء آخر فقبول القسمة بمعنى فرض شيء غير شيء باعتبار مساواة بعض منه لما هو أصغر منه ولولا ذلك لم يكن قابلا لها ومجرد هذه المساواة كافية في القسمة المذكورة أو يقال : إِنَّ كون المقدار بحيث ينفرض فيه شيء غير شيء إنما هو لأجل عدم مساواة مجموعه من حيث هو لبعضه الذي يفرضه العقل أو لا شيئاً إذ لولا ذلك لم يمكنه أن يفرض فيه شيئاً فيفرض بعده شيئاً آخر ومجرد هذه. قوله : ( وأعراضها الذاتية أي اللاحقة لذاتها فلا ينافي كون الثالثة فرع الأولى أو العكس لأنها واسطة في الثبوت كما يدل عليه بيان الفرعية . قوله : ( إذا فرض أجزاء في كم أي يمكن حصولها فيه سواء كانت حاصلة بالفعل كما في العدد أو لا كما في المقدار، فهذه الأجزاء لا تكون متفاوتة في المقدار فإن كان بإزاء كل جزء مفروض في كم جزء في كم آخر كانا متساويين، وإن لا يوجد كان أحدهما زائداً والآخر ناقصاً. فاندفع ما قيل : لا نسلم أنه إذا وجد في المقدار بإزاء كل جزء مفروض في أحدهما جزء في الآخر يكونان متساويين لجواز أن تكون مقادير الأجزاء متفاوتة . قوله : ( الثالثة المساواة فيه إشكال، وهو أنَّ الحكم بالمساواة قد يكون بملاحظة الوحدة خارجة عن الكم بقسميه ومن أدرجها في العدد يلزمه أن لا يجعل العدد مطلقاً مندرجاً التي هي تحت الكم. قوله : ( وهو فرع الخاصة الأولى يجوز أن يكون تذكير الضمير باعتبار الخبر فلا يحتاج حينئذ إلى التأويل الذي ذكره الشارح، ثم إِنَّ الفرعية باعتبار أنَّ الخاصة الأولى واسطة في الثبوت لا العروض فلا ينافي كونها عرضاً ذاتيا، على أنَّ الواسطة في العروض إنما ينافي أولية العرض لا كونه عرضاً ذاتياً الذي هو المدعى هاهنا لجواز أن يكون عرض أولى واسطة في عروض عرض ذاتي . قوله: (لأنه إذا فرض أجزاء في كم إلخ) فيه بحث وهو أنَّ المقادير يمكن أن تفرض متفاوتة الأجزاء فإن شرط تساويها تنقل الكلام إلى ذلك التساوي وهلم جرا. فالظاهر أنَّ هذا لا يجري في المقادير بل في الأعداد، وإن أوهم لفظ الفرض بكونه في المقادير بناء على أن الأجزاء بالفعل متحققة في العدد فلا احتياج إلى الفرض لكن المراد بالفرض حينئذ هو الملاحظة ليس إلا وبالجملة الفرض المطابق للواقع مجامع للفعل.
The third (property): equality and its counterparts, namely increase and decrease. For the intellect, when it observes magnitudes or numbers without observing anything else with them, can judge between them by equality, increase, or decrease; and when it observes something else without observing with it a number or a magnitude, it cannot judge any of them. So the acceptance of these matters is among the properties of quantities and their essential accidents. And it (i.e., this mentioned thing which is the third property) is a branch of the first property, because if parts are assumed in a quantity, then either corresponding to every assumed part in that quantity there is an assumed part in another quantity, or more, or less—then the first quantity is characterized by equality, decrease, or increase in comparison to the second quantity. And some reversed it, making the acceptance of division a branch of the acceptance of equality and inequality. Its justification is that estimation divides the magnitude only when it observes another smaller magnitude, so it assumes in it what equals it, which is something, and the excess remains, which is something else. So acceptance of division in the sense of assuming something other than something is due to the equality of a part of it to what is smaller than it; and were it not for that, it would not be receptive to it. And mere equality is sufficient for the mentioned division. Or it is said: the magnitude being such that something other than something can be assumed in it is only because its totality as such is not equal to the part that the intellect assumes; for were it not so, it could not assume something in it and then assume something else after it. And mere this... His statement: 'and their essential accidents' means those that follow their essence, so it does not contradict the third being a branch of the first or vice versa, because it is a mediating factor in proof (wāsita fī al-thubūt), as the explanation of branching indicates. His statement: 'if parts are assumed in a quantity' means they can obtain in it, whether they obtain actually as in number or not as in magnitude, and these parts do not differ in magnitude. For if corresponding to every assumed part in a quantity there is a part in another quantity, they are equal; and if not, one is greater and the other lesser. Thus what was said is repelled: 'we do not concede that if there exists in a magnitude corresponding to every assumed part in one of them a part in the other, they are equal, because the magnitudes of the parts could differ.' His statement: 'the third (property): equality' has a problem, namely that judgment of equality may be by observing a unit external to quantity in both its divisions, and whoever includes it in number must not make number as such included under quantity. His statement: 'and it is a branch of the first property'—the pronoun may be masculine because of the predicate, so then no need for the interpretation that the commentator mentioned. Then branching is in the sense that the first property is a mediating factor in proof (wāsita fī al-thubūt), not in attribution (al-ʿurūḍ), so it does not contradict its being an essential accident; for the meditator in attribution only contradicts the priority of the accident, not its being an essential accident, which is the claim here, because a prior accident can be a mediator in the attribution of an essential accident. His statement: 'because if parts are assumed in a quantity, etc.'—there is a discussion in it, namely that magnitudes can be assumed to have different parts; if the condition of their equality is required, the discussion moves to that equality, and so on. So apparently this does not apply to magnitudes but to numbers, although the word 'assumption' suggests it applies to magnitudes, on the basis that parts are actually realized in number so there is no need for assumption—but here the meaning of assumption is only observation. In short, assumption conforming to reality is compatible with actuality.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage defines the third property of quantity: the intellect can judge equality, increase, or decrease when considering magnitudes or numbers alone, but not when considering other things without quantity. This shows that such judgments are essential accidents of quantity. The passage then discusses whether this third property is a branch of the first property (acceptance of division). The author argues it is: if parts are assumed in one quantity and compared to another, equality or inequality results. Some reverse this, making division dependent on equality. The justification is that estimation divides a magnitude by comparing it to a smaller one, assuming a part equal to it—division assumes inequality of the whole and part. The commentary clarifies that 'essential accidents' means properties following the essence, so branching does not contradict essentiality; the first property is a mediating factor in proof, not in attribution. There is a discussion about parts in magnitudes: they can be assumed to differ in size, but the argument for equality requires equal parts, which is fine. A problem is raised: equality can be judged by a unit outside quantity, but that is addressed. The branching is further explained: the pronoun in 'it is a branch' may be masculine, avoiding an interpretation. Finally, a discussion: magnitudes can be assumed with different parts, so the argument works better for numbers, but 'assumption' here means observation, not actual composition.

Key Terms
المساواة
الزيادة
النقصان
الخاصة الأولى
العرض الذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Equality and inequality can be judged for any attributes, not just quantities.
Correction: The text states that only when observing magnitudes or numbers without anything else can the intellect judge equality; otherwise it cannot.

Checkpoint
Why is the third property of quantity (equality, increase, decrease) considered a branch of the first property (acceptance of division)?
A) Because the third property is derived from the first by negation.
B) Because comparing two quantities requires assuming parts in each, which is the first property.
C) Because the intellect cannot judge equality without dividing quantities.
D) Because the first property is more fundamental in logic.

Key Takeaway
The third property of quantity (equality, increase, decrease) is an essential accident and depends on the first property (acceptance of division) for its application.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you judged yourself as 'less than' someone else. According to this lesson, such a judgment is only valid if you are comparing quantities. Are you comparing quantities of something that can actually be measured? What if you are comparing qualities that cannot be measured? Does that realization change how you view that comparison? What can you do to stop comparing yourself in ways that are not valid?
?
The third property depends on the first: assuming parts. In your spiritual life, do you assume 'parts' of your worship (e.g., number of rak'at, days fasted) to judge your standing before Allah? Could this focus on quantity distract you from the quality of your devotion? What concrete step can you take this week to shift your focus from counting deeds to improving their sincerity and presence of heart?

The Reversal: Is Division a Branch of Equality?
Presents and justifies the reversed relationship between division and equality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative view that equality and inequality precede division, and the reasoning behind it.

Core Idea
Some scholars argued that division depends on equality, not the other way around.

The previous lesson established that the third property branches from the first. However, some scholars reversed this relationship, claiming that acceptance of division is actually a branch of equality and inequality. The passage now examines this alternative view, providing justifications and discussing implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative view
Read the lines where the text says 'And some reversed it...' Understand the claim: division is a branch of equality and inequality.
Note the contrasting position.
2
Understand the first justification
The first justification involves estimation: to divide a magnitude, the mind observes a smaller magnitude, assumes a part equal to it, and then the excess remains. Thus division relies on equality.
Equality is used to define the smaller part.
3
Understand the second justification
The second justification states that a magnitude can be divided into different parts only because its whole is not equal to any one part; if it were equal, no division would occur.
Inequality is the basis for division.
4
Reflect on the discussion about magnitudes vs numbers
The passage notes that the assumption of parts works better for numbers (which have actual parts) than for magnitudes (where parts can be arbitrarily unequal). It concludes that 'assumption' here means mental observation, not actual composition.
This clarifies the scope of the argument.

Commentary
This passage defines the third property of quantity: the intellect can judge equality, increase, or decrease when considering magnitudes or numbers alone, but not when considering other things without quantity. This shows that such judgments are essential accidents of quantity. The passage then discusses whether this third property is a branch of the first property (acceptance of division). The author argues it is: if parts are assumed in one quantity and compared to another, equality or inequality results. Some reverse this, making division dependent on equality. The commentary clarifies that 'essential accidents' means properties following the essence, so branching does not contradict essentiality; the first property is a mediating factor in proof, not in attribution. There is a discussion about parts in magnitudes: they can be assumed to differ in size, but the argument for equality requires equal parts, which is fine. A problem is raised: equality can be judged by a unit outside quantity, but that is addressed. The branching is further explained: the pronoun in 'it is a branch' may be masculine, avoiding an interpretation. Finally, a discussion: magnitudes can be assumed with different parts, so the argument works better for numbers, but 'assumption' here means observation, not actual composition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Division must always logically precede equality, because you need parts to compare.
Correction: The passage shows that equality can be prior: estimation uses equality to define a part, and the whole's inequality to its parts enables division.

Checkpoint
According to the reversed view, what is the basis for dividing a magnitude?
A) The assumption of parts without any comparison.
B) The equality of a part to a smaller magnitude and the inequality of the whole to its parts.
C) The actual composition of the magnitude into equal parts.
D) The observation of a unit external to quantity.

Key Takeaway
Some scholars argue that equality and inequality precede division, as estimation uses equality to divide magnitudes and inequality makes division possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that scholars debated even the order of basic logical relations. When you find yourself certain that 'A depends on B', what would it take for you to genuinely consider the opposite? Write down one conviction you hold and try to argue the reverse for a moment.
?
If equality (musāwāt) precedes division, then unity (tawḥīd) may be the basis for multiplicity in your own life. In what areas do you act as if multiplicity comes first—your tasks, worries, or desires—rather than grounding them in the oneness of Allah? Identify one step to reprioritize.
?
The passage uses estimation (wahm) to explain how division works. How often do you 'estimate' your own capabilities or situations without checking reality? Recall a time your estimation misled you. How can you submit your estimations to the truth of the source, even if it upsets your assumed order?

Branching Does Not Negate Essential Accidents
Commentary clarifying that branching through a mediator in proof does not negate essentiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a property can be a branch of another (via mediator in proof) while still being an essential accident.

Core Idea
Branching through a mediator in proof preserves essentiality.

This commentary addresses a potential confusion: if one property is derived from another, does that make it accidental? The author clarifies that branching through a mediator in proof (wāsiṭa fī al-thubūt) does not negate its essential accidental nature. We will explore the distinction between mediator in proof and mediator in attribution.

Guided Path
1
Understand essential accidents
Recall that essential accidents (aʿrāḍ dhātiyya) are properties that follow the essence of a thing. The commentary builds on this concept.
Essential accidents are not mere external additions; they are necessary concomitants.
2
Distinguish mediators
Grasp the key distinction: a mediator in proof (wāsiṭa fī al-thubūt) is used to establish the existence of a property, while a mediator in attribution (wāsiṭa fī al-ʿurūḍ) determines how a property is attributed. The passage argues that branching only involves the former.
This distinction is crucial for understanding why branching does not affect essentiality.
3
Apply to the branching scenario
See how the first property being a mediator in proof for the third does not make the third accidental. The commentary notes that even if a primary accident mediates in attribution, it only negates priority, not essentiality.
The argument shows that essentiality is about following the essence, not about being primary or derived.

قوله : ( وأعراضها (الذاتية أي اللاحقة لذاتها فلا ينافي كون الثالثة فرع الأولى أو العكس لأنها واسطة في الثبوت كما يدل عليه بيان الفرعية . قوله : ( وهو فرع الخاصة (الأولى) يجوز أن يكون تذكير الضمير باعتبار الخبر فلا يحتاج حينئذ إلى التأويل الذي ذكره الشارح، ثم إِنَّ الفرعية باعتبار أنَّ الخاصة الأولى واسطة في الثبوت لا العروض فلا ينافي كونها عرضاً ذاتيا، على أنَّ الواسطة في العروض إنما ينافي أولية العرض لا كونه عرضاً ذاتياً الذي هو المدعى هاهنا لجواز أن يكون عرض أولى واسطة في عروض عرض ذاتي .
His statement: '(And their essential accidents)' i.e. those that follow their essence, so it does not negate the third being a branch of the first or the reverse, because it is a mediator in proof (wāsiṭa fī al-thubūt), as the explanation of branching shows. His statement: '(And it is a branch of the first property)' – it is permissible that the masculine pronoun be considered as referring to the predicate, so there is no need then for the interpretation that the commentator mentioned. Then indeed the branching (al-far'iyya) is considered as the first property being a mediator in proof, not in attribution (al-'urūḍ), so it does not negate it being an essential accident. Moreover, the mediator in attribution only negates the priority of the accident, not its being an essential accident, which is what is claimed here, because it is possible that a primary accident be a mediator in the attribution of an essential accident.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining two points. First, the phrase 'essential accidents' means those that follow the essence, so the third property being a branch of the first (or vice versa) does not negate its essentiality, because the first property is a mediator in proof (wāsiṭa fī al-thubūt) not in attribution (al-'urūḍ). Second, in the statement 'it is a branch of the first property', the masculine pronoun may be an reference to the predicate, avoiding the need for interpretive stretching. The key argument is that branching (far'iyya) is based on the first property being a mediator in proof, not in attribution, so it remains an essential accident. A mediator in attribution would negate priority but not essentiality.

واسطة في العروض
عرض ذاتي

خاصة أولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If one property is a branch of another, it cannot be essential.
Correction: Branching does not negate essentiality when the branch is mediated through proof, not through attribution.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the role of the first property in relation to the third property's essentiality?
A) The first property negates the essential nature of the third.
B) The first property is a mediator in attribution, making the third accidental.
C) The first property is a mediator in proof, so the third remains essential.
D) The first property has no relation to the third.

Key Takeaway
Essential accidents can be branches of other properties through mediation in proof without losing their essential status.

The Condition of Parts in Quantity: Equality and the Objection of Varying Magnitudes
Main rule and objection about part magnitudes in quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why assumed parts in a quantity must not vary in magnitude for equality comparison, and how this reasoning applies differently to numbers and magnitudes.

Core Idea
For equality comparison, assumed parts must themselves be equal in magnitude.

This lesson unpacks a key condition in reasoning about quantities: when we assume parts in a quantity, those parts must not vary in magnitude. The commentary then addresses an objection that parts could have different magnitudes, and clarifies that the reasoning applies specifically to numbers, where parts are actual, not to continuous magnitudes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the structure: condition → proof → objection → repulsion → discussion.
2
Identify the main condition
Find the statement that assumed parts in a quantity are not varying in magnitude. Understand that this is a precondition for equality comparison.
The condition is explicit: 'these parts are not varying in magnitude'.
3
Understand the objection and its rebuttal
The objection claims parts can have different magnitudes, undermining equality. The rebuttal shows the condition itself eliminates that possibility.
The rebuttal is that if parts are assumed equal, the objection is irrelevant.
4
Examine the commentator's discussion
The commentator notes that the reasoning works for numbers (actual parts) but not for magnitudes (potential parts). Understand the role of 'assumption' as 'observation'.
This clarifies the scope of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( إذا فرض أجزاء في كم أي يمكن حصولها فيه سواء كانت حاصلة بالفعل كما في العدد أو لا كما في المقدار، فهذه الأجزاء لا تكون متفاوتة في المقدار فإن كان بإزاء كل جزء مفروض في كم جزء في كم ،آخر كانا متساويين، وإن لا يوجد كان أحدهما زائداً والآخر ناقصاً. فاندفع ما قيل : لا نسلم أنه إذا وجد في المقدار بإزاء كل جزء مفروض في أحدهما جزء في الآخر يكونان متساويين لجواز أن تكون مقادير الأجزاء متفاوتة . قوله: (لأنه إذا فرض أجزاء في كم إلخ) فيه بحث وهو أنَّ المقادير يمكن أن تفرض متفاوتة الأجزاء فإن شرط تساويها تنقل الكلام إلى ذلك التساوي وهلم جرا. فالظاهر أنَّ هذا لا يجري في المقادير بل في الأعداد، وإن أوهم لفظ الفرض بكونه في المقادير بناء على أن الأجزاء بالفعل متحققة في العدد فلا احتياج إلى الفرض لكن المراد بالفرض حينئذ هو الملاحظة ليس إلا وبالجملة الفرض المطابق للواقع مجامع للفعل.
His statement: '(If parts are assumed in a quantity)' i.e. they can occur in it, whether they actually exist as in number or not as in magnitude. So these parts are not varying in magnitude. For if opposite every assumed part in one quantity there is a part in another quantity, they are equal; and if not, one is excess and the other deficient. So is repelled what was said: 'We do not accept that when there exists in a magnitude opposite every assumed part in one of them a part in the other, they are equal, because it is possible that the magnitudes of the parts vary.' His statement: '(Because if parts are assumed in a quantity etc.)' contains a discussion, which is that magnitudes can be assumed with varying parts. For if their equality is conditioned, the discussion shifts to that equality and so on. So it is apparent that this does not apply to magnitudes but to numbers, although the term 'assumption' suggests it applies to magnitudes, based on the fact that parts actually exist in numbers so there is no need for assumption. But the meaning of 'assumption' then is only observation. In short, the assumption that conforms to reality coincides with actuality.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This commentary clarifies the condition for parts when assuming parts in a quantity. The parts must not vary in magnitude. If each assumed part in one quantity has a corresponding part in another, the quantities are equal; otherwise one is larger. This refutes the objection that parts might have different magnitudes, making equality uncertain. The commentator then discusses the phrase 'because if parts are assumed' and notes that magnitudes can be assumed with varying parts; if equality of parts is a condition, the discussion shifts to that equality. Ultimately, this reasoning applies to numbers, not magnitudes, because in numbers parts actually exist and need not be assumed. The term 'assumption' here really means 'observation'.

Misconception: One might think that assumed parts can have different magnitudes while still comparing quantities fairly.
Correction: The condition requires that the assumed parts themselves are equal in magnitude; otherwise the comparison is invalid.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why does the objection about unequal part magnitudes fail?
A) The magnitudes of parts are always equal.
B) The condition of equality makes the comparison valid only for numbers, not for measures.
C) The assumption is imaginary, not actual.

Key Takeaway
The equality of quantities via parts depends on the parts themselves being equal in magnitude; this reasoning works only when parts are actual (as in numbers).

Equality and Inequality as Branches of Division
Defines the third property and presents two views on its relation to division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third property of quantity (equality/inequality) is derived from the first property (accepting division), and also the alternative view that reverses this dependency with its justifications.

Core Idea
Equality and inequality arise from comparing assumed parts in quantities.

In this passage, the author explores the relationship between two fundamental properties of quantity: the ability to be divided and the ability to be compared as equal or unequal. He presents the standard view that equality/inequality stems from division, then offers a rival perspective that reverses the dependency, along with two supporting justifications. Understanding this debate sharpens our grasp of how Islamic philosophers and theologians conceptualized quantity and its essential accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its structure. Then read the translation. Notice that the passage presents two different views about the relationship between division and comparison.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ومنهم من عكس' which indicates a reversal of the standard view.
2
Identify the two views
The passage first states that the third property (equality/inequality) is a branch of the first property (accepting division). Then it says some scholars reversed this, making division a branch of equality/inequality. Locate these two positions in the text.
3
Analyze the justifications for the reversed view
The reversed view is supported by two justifications. The first relies on equality when comparing a magnitude to a smaller one; the second relies on inequality between the whole and its part. Understand how each justification uses equality or inequality to explain division.
The justifications involve the role of the imagination (الوهم) in dividing magnitudes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الأول : خواص الكم وأقسامه الكميات وأعراضها الذاتية (وهو) أي هذا المذكور الذي هو الخاصة الثالثة (فرع الخاصة الأولى لأنه إذا فرض أجزاء) في كم ) فإما أن يوجد بإزاء كل جزء) مفروض في ذلك الكم ( جزء) مفروض في كم آخر ( أو أكثر أو أقل ) فيتصف حينئذ الكم الأول بالمساواة أو بالنقصان أو بالزيادة مقيساً إلى الكم الثاني. ومنهم من ومنهم من عكس فجعل قبول القسمة فرعاً لقبول المساواة واللامساواة وتوجيهه أن يقال : إن الوهم إنما يقسم المقدار إذا لاحظ مقداراً آخر أصغر منه فيفرض فيه ما يساويه وهو شيء ويبقى الفضل وهو شيء آخر فقبول القسمة بمعنى فرض شيء غير شيء باعتبار مساواة بعض منه لما هو أصغر منه ولولا ذلك لم يكن قابلا لها ومجرد هذه المساواة كافية في القسمة المذكورة أو يقال : إِنَّ كون المقدار بحيث ينفرض فيه شيء غير شيء إنما هو لأجل عدم مساواة مجموعه من حيث هو لبعضه الذي يفرضه العقل أو لا شيئاً إذ لولا ذلك لم يمكنه أن يفرض فيه شيئاً فيفرض بعده شيئاً آخر ومجرد هذه اللامساواة كافية في قبول القسمة الوهمية
The second observatory - the first purpose: Properties of quantity and its divisions. Quantities and their essential accidents (and it) i.e. this mentioned which is the third property (is a branch of the first property, because if parts are assumed) in a quantity, then either there exists opposite every assumed part in that quantity a part assumed in another quantity, or more, or less; then the first quantity is described as equality, deficiency, or excess measured against the second quantity. And some of them reversed, making acceptance of division a branch of acceptance of equality and inequality. And its direction is to say: The imagination only divides the magnitude when it observes another magnitude smaller than it, so it assumes in it what equals it, which is a thing, and the remainder remains, which is another thing. So acceptance of division means assuming something other than something by virtue of equality of part of it to what is smaller than it, and were it not for that it would not be susceptible to it. And mere this equality is sufficient for the mentioned division. Or it is said: The magnitude being such that something other than something can be assumed in it is only because of the lack of equality of its totality as such to some of it which the mind assumes, or nothing, since were it not for that it could not assume something in it and then assume afterwards another thing. And mere this inequality is sufficient for acceptance of imaginary division.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage defines the third property of quantity (equality/inequality) as a branch of the first property (accepting division). It begins by stating that when parts are assumed in one quantity, relative to another quantity the first is equal, greater, or less. Then it mentions an alternative view that reverses the relationship, making acceptance of division a branch of equality/inequality. Two justifications are given for this reversed view: first, that division requires noting a smaller magnitude, assuming equality to part of it, leaving a remainder; second, that division is possible because the whole is not equal to its part. The passage concludes that mere inequality suffices for imaginary division.

Correction: The passage shows that equality/inequality is either a branch of division (standard view) or division is a branch of equality/inequality (reversed view). They are intrinsically linked.

Al-Razi's Objections to Defining Quantity
Al-Razi's objections to defining quantity by equality/inequality and by division, with responses and a refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's two objections to defining quantity (al-kamm) and how scholars responded.

Core Idea
Defining quantity by equality or division leads to circularity or incompleteness.

Imam al-Razi challenges two common definitions of quantity: one based on equality/inequality and another on acceptance of division. These objections force us to think carefully about what quantity is and how it can be defined. The passage presents al-Razi's arguments and the scholarly responses, including a critique of a later interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Razi's first objection
Read the part about equality (al-musāwāh) and inequality. Why does al-Razi say it leads to a vicious circle (al-dawr)?
Equality is defined as unity in quantity, so defining quantity by equality would be circular.
2
Examine the response from al-Mabāhith al-Mashriqiyya
The response argues that equality and inequality are perceived by the senses, breaking the circularity. Understand how the senses and intellect work together.
Quantity is perceived together with its subject; the mind distinguishes them.
3
Analyze al-Razi's second objection
The second objection is about defining quantity by acceptance of division (qubūl al-qisma). Why does al-Razi say it applies only to continuous quantity?
Division seems to apply only to continuous quantities like lines or surfaces, not discrete ones like numbers.
4
Understand the resolution on division
The commentator points out that the correct view is to consider suppositional division (al-qisma al-fardiyya) which covers both continuous and discrete quantities. This makes the definition valid.
The author's interpretation involving 'divisive division' (al-qisma al-infikākiyya) is rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام الرازي: لا يمكن تعريف الكم بالمساواة والمفاوتة لأن المساواة لا تعرف إلا بأنها اتحاد في الكم فيلزم الدور وذكر في المباحث المشرقية أنه يمكن أن يجاب عنه بأنَّ المساواة واللامساواة مما يدرك بالحس والكم لا يناله الحس مفرداً، بل إنما يناله مع المتكمم تناولاً واحداً ثم إنَّ العقل يجتهد في تمييز أحد المفهومين عن الآخر فلهذا يمكن تعريف ذلك المعقول بهذا المحسوس يعني وهذا المحسوس مستغن عن التعريف وإمكان أخذه في تعريفه لا يقتضي توقف معرفته عليه (ولا) يمكن أيضاً تعريف الكم ( بقبول القسمة لأنه يختص بالمتصل منه ) قد عرفت وجه الاختصاص بالمتصل وعدم تناوله للمنفصل بالقيد الذي زيد في مفهوم القسمة الوهمية كما صرح به في المباحث وأشير إليه في الملخص وعرفت أيضاً أن الصواب عدم اعتبار ذلك القيد وأنَّ القسمة الفرضية تتناول الكم بقسميه معاً فيجوز تعريفه : بقبول هذه القسمة وأما توجيه المصنف كلام الإمام بقوله : ( كأنه أخذ القسمة الانفكاكية) فليس بشيء إذ قد تبين آنفاً أنَّ الكم المتصل لا يقبل القسمة الانفكاكية وقد قرره الإمام في كتابيه تقريراً واضحاً فكيف يتصور اختصاص قبول
Imam al-Razi said: It is not possible to define quantity (al-kamm) by equality and inequality because equality is not known except as unity in quantity, so a vicious circle (al-dawr) follows. And it was mentioned in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya that it can be answered that equality and inequality are perceived by sense, and quantity is not perceived by sense alone, but rather it is perceived together with the quantified (al-mutakammam) in a single apprehension. Then the mind strives to distinguish one concept from the other, so for this reason that intellectual can be defined by this sensible, meaning that this sensible is independent of definition, and the possibility of taking it in its definition does not require that its knowledge be dependent on it. And it is also not possible to define quantity by acceptance of division because it is specific to the continuous part of it. You have known the aspect of specificity to the continuous and its not encompassing the discrete by the condition that was added in the concept of imaginary division, as was stated in al-Mabahith and indicated in al-Mulakhkhas. And you also know that the correct view is not to consider that condition, and that suppositional division covers both divisions of quantity, so it is permissible to define it by acceptance of this division. As for the author's interpretation of Imam's statement by saying: 'It is as if he took the divisive division (al-qisma al-infikākiyya)', it is nothing, since it has just become clear that continuous quantity does not accept divisive division, and the Imam confirmed it clearly in his two books. So how can one imagine the specificity of acceptance...
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents a key objection by Imam al-Razi against defining quantity (al-kamm) by equality/inequality or by acceptance of division. For the first, he argues circularity: equality is defined as unity in quantity, so defining quantity by equality would be circular. A response from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya is given: equality/inequality are sensed, and quantity is sensed together with its subject (al-mutakammam) in one apprehension; the mind then distinguishes them, so quantity can be defined by what is sensible, which is independent. For the second, defining by division fails because it applies only to continuous quantity. The commentator clarifies that the correct view is not to restrict division to imaginary division with a condition; rather, suppositional division covers both continuous and discrete, so defining by division is possible. The author's attempt to interpret al-Razi as referring to 'divisive division' (al-qisma al-infikākiyya) is rejected because continuous quantity does not accept such division.

مساواة
مفاوتة
حس
متكمم
قبول القسمة
متصل
منفصل
قسمة وهمية
قسمة انفكاكية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think equality is a primitive, self-evident notion that can be used without circularity.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that equality depends on quantity, so using it to define quantity is circular. The response argues equality is sensible, breaking the dependence.

Checkpoint
What is the core problem with defining quantity by equality/inequality, according to Imam al-Razi?
A) Equality and inequality are not essential properties.
B) Equality is defined as unity in quantity, leading to circularity.
C) Equality is not perceived by the senses.
D) Quantity cannot be defined at all.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's objections challenge the definitions of quantity, but through careful analysis they can be resolved without circularity or incompleteness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage shows that what seems like a circular definition can be resolved by distinguishing between sense and intellect. In your own spiritual life, have you ever relied on a superficial understanding of a concept (like ‘iman or tawakkul) that later required deeper intellectual reflection to avoid inconsistency? What would it take for you to pursue that clarity?

Essentiality of Properties and Two Types of Equality
Sanusi explains essentiality of properties and distinguishes two types of equality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why each property of quantity is essential to it and how numerical equality differs from magnitude equality.

Core Idea
Properties of quantity are inseparable yet conceptually distinct; equality comes in two forms: numerical and magnitude.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a key principle: each property of quantity, such as divisibility and equality, is an essential accident of quantity itself, even though they are concomitant. He then distinguishes between two kinds of equality and inequality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the two main points: essentiality and types of equality.
Notice the structure: first qawl and second qawl.
2
Identify the essentiality claim
Observe that the commentator says each property is an accident of quantity by essence (li-dhatihi), even if they are concomitant. Think about what 'concomitant' means.
The example: we can conceive division without equality, and equality without division.
3
Distinguish the two types of equality
The second qawl distinguishes equality in magnitude from equality in numbers. Magnitude equality does not require assuming parts, while numerical equality does.
This is a key doctrinal point.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it shows that quantity has independent properties that do not always depend on divisibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والظاهر) والأظهر أن كل واحد من الخواص عارضة للكم لذاته، وإن كانت متلازمة فإنا نعقل الانقسام مع الغفلة عن المساواة واللامساواة وكذا نعقل المساواة والمفاوتة مع الغفلة عن القسمة . قوله : ( إنما هو في المساواة إلخ) وأما المساواة واللامساواة المقدارية فلا يحتاج فيه إلى فرض الأجزاء. قوله : ( أحد المفهومين) أي الكم والمتكمم . قوله : (يمكن تعريف ذلك المعقول أي الكم منفرداً عن معروضه بهذا المحسوس، أي بالكم المعلوم بالمشاهدة مع المتكمم فالمعرف الكم المعقول والمأخوذ في تعريفه الكم المعلوم بالحس مع المتكمم ، لأن معرفته كاف في معرفة المساواة والمفاوتة. قوله : ( بالقيد الذي زيد إلخ ) وهو لا يزال كذلك أبداً . قوله : ( واللامساواة مما يدرك بالحس المراد باللامساواة الزيادة والنقصان، وهما وجوديان يمكن أن يدركا بالحس. قوله : (بل إنما يناله مع المتكمم تناولاً واحداً بخلاف المساواة مثلاً، فإنها وإن أحس بها مع المحل لكن بإحساسين لا بإحساس واحد وسيجيء تحقيقه في أول بحث المبصرات .
His statement: '(And it is apparent)' – and the more apparent is that each one of the properties is an accident of quantity by essence (li-dhātihi), even if they are concomitant. For we understand division while being heedless of equality and inequality, and similarly we understand equality and inequality while being heedless of division. His statement: '(It is only in equality etc.)' – as for equality and inequality in magnitude (al-miqdāriyya), they do not require assumption of parts. His statement: '(One of the two concepts)' i.e. quantity and the quantified (al-mutakammam). His statement: '(It is possible to define that intellectual)' i.e. quantity separate from its subject by this sensible, i.e. by the quantity known by observation together with the quantified. So the definer is the intellectual quantity, and what is taken in its definition is the quantity known by sense together with the quantified, because its knowledge is sufficient for knowing equality and inequality. His statement: '(By the condition that was added etc.)' – and it is still like that forever. His statement: '(And inequality is something perceived by sense)' – by inequality is meant increase and deficiency, both of which are existential and can be perceived by sense. His statement: '(Rather, it is attained together with the quantified in a single apprehension, unlike equality, for example, for it, even if sensed with the subject, is with two sensations, not one. And its verification will come at the beginning of the discussion of visible things (al-mubsirāt).'
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage clarifies several points in the commentary. First, each property (division, equality/inequality) is an essential accident of quantity, even if they are concomitant; we can conceive one without the other. Second, equality and inequality in magnitude (al-miqdāriyya) do not require assumption of parts, unlike numerical equality. Third, the 'two concepts' are quantity itself and the quantified subject. Fourth, the response to al-Razi's circularity objection: defining intellectual quantity by sensible quantity (which is perceived with the quantified) is valid because the sensible is independent. Fifth, the added condition in imaginary division is rejected; it is not needed. Sixth, inequality (excess and deficiency) is existential and perceivable by sense. Finally, quantity with its subject is perceived in a single act, unlike equality which requires two sensations (comparing two quantities).

Key Terms
عارضة للكم لذاته
متلازمة
مساواة مقدارية
مساواة عددية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that equality and inequality always depend on division (i.e., comparing parts).
Correction: The commentary clarifies that equality and inequality in magnitude do not require assuming parts; they are directly perceived.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what distinguishes equality in magnitude from equality in numbers?
A) They are the same.
B) Magnitude equality requires assumption of parts, numerical does not.
C) Numerical equality requires assumption of parts, magnitude equality does not.
D) Both require assumption of parts.

Key Takeaway
Each property of quantity is essential to it, and magnitude equality is conceptually independent of numerical equality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily judgments, do you tend to measure others by numerical standards (e.g., wealth, followers) or by intrinsic magnitude (e.g., character, sincerity)? How does this lesson's distinction challenge your habit?
?
Consider a belief you hold that seems always connected to another (like faith and action). Have you ever conflated them? What would it mean to distinguish them conceptually without separating them practically, as the lesson does with division and equality?

Defining Quantity and Perceiving Inequality
Sanusi explains definition of quantity via sensible and clarifies inequality as existential and perceived.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how quantity can be defined via sensible quantity and how inequality is existential and perceived by sense.

Core Idea
Quantity is known through sensible perception; inequality is a real quality sensed in a single act.

This part of the commentary addresses a potential circularity in defining quantity and discusses the nature of inequality. The commentator argues that defining intellectual quantity by sensible quantity is valid, and that inequality is an existential property perceived by sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the flow: definition via sensible, rejection of added condition, inequality as sensed, perception distinction.
2
Understand the definition response
The commentator responds to an objection: defining intellectual quantity by sensible quantity is not circular because sensible quantity is independent and known by perception.
Sensible quantity = quantity known by observation with its subject.
3
Grasp the nature of inequality
Inequality (increase/deficiency) is presented as existential (wujudiyyan) and thus perceivable by sense.
This counters any notion that inequality is merely privative.
4
Note the perception distinction
Quantity with its subject is perceived in a single act, whereas equality requires two sensations (comparing two quantities).
This difference is a key insight.

Numerical vs Quantitative Equality and Inequality
Distinguishes numerical vs quantitative equality/inequality and their relation to imaginary division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between numerical and quantitative equality/inequality and why only inequality is required for imaginary division.

Core Idea
Inequality suffices for imaginary division; quantitative equality needs no parts.

In this passage, the author refines our understanding of equality and inequality as properties of quantity. He distinguishes two types: numerical and quantitative. The key insight is that while numerical equality/inequality involves supposing parts, quantitative equality/inequality does not. This clarifies a potential confusion in earlier discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get the overall flow. Note the technical terms.
The passage is dense with logical distinctions.
2
Identify the two types of equality/inequality
Locate where the author distinguishes numerical from quantitative equality/inequality. Notice that they are opposites in some way.
The phrase 'عكسه' indicates an inversion.
3
Understand the condition for imaginary division
Pay attention to the claim that inequality alone is sufficient for accepting imaginary division. Contrast this with the need for equality.
This is a key logical point.
4
Reflect on the role of parts
Note the final statement: quantitative equality/inequality does not require supposing parts, whereas numerical does. Ask yourself why.
This resolves a potential misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللامساواة كافية في قبول القسمة الوهمية والظاهر أنَّ ما في الكتاب إنما هو في المساواة واللامساواة العددية وإنّ عكسه إنما هو في المساواة واللامساواة المقدارية . قوله : ( والظاهر) والأظهر أن كل واحد من الخواص عارضة للكم لذاته، وإن كانت عن اعتبار مساواة جزء لما هو أصغر منه وعدم مساواة متلازمة فإنا نعقل الانقسام مع الغفلة عن القسمة . المجموع للبعض وكذا نعقل المساواة والمفاوتة مع قوله : ( إنما هو في المساواة إلخ) وأما المساواة واللامساواة المقدارية فلا يحتاج فيه إلى فرض الأجزاء.
Inequality is sufficient for the acceptance of imaginary division. And it appears that what is in the book is only about numerical equality and inequality, and its opposite is only about quantitative equality and inequality. He said: (And it appears) and it is more apparent that each one of the properties is an accident of quantity in itself, even if they are connected by considering the equality of a part to what is smaller than it and the inequality are concomitant, for we understand division while heedless of division. The totality to the part, and likewise we understand equality and inequality. With his saying: (It is only about equality etc.) And as for quantitative equality and inequality, it does not need the supposition of parts.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. It begins by stating that inequality is sufficient for accepting imaginary division (a property of quantity). Then it clarifies that what is in the book (al-Mawaqif) concerns numerical equality/inequality, while its opposite concerns quantitative equality/inequality. The commentator then explains that each property is an essential accident of quantity, even though equality and inequality are correlated. We understand division even without thinking about division, and likewise we understand equality and inequality. Finally, it states that quantitative equality/inequality does not require the supposition of parts, unlike numerical equality/inequality which does.

Correction: Quantitative equality/inequality does not require supposing parts; only numerical equality/inequality does.

Checkpoint

A) Numerical equality/inequality
B) Quantitative equality/inequality
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Quantitative equality/inequality is distinct from numerical equality/inequality because it does not require supposing parts.

Al-Razi's Objection and the Sense Perception Response
Al-Razi's objection and the response using sense perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's circularity objection to defining quantity by equality and inequality, and the response that sense perception provides a non-circular foundation.

Core Idea
Sense perception breaks the circularity: equality is sensed directly, not defined by quantity.

Al-Razi pointed out a logical problem: if we define quantity by equality, but equality itself is defined as unity in quantity, we get a vicious circle. The response from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya offers a clever way out: equality and inequality are directly perceived by the senses, so they can serve as a foundation without requiring prior knowledge of quantity. This lesson walks through the objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal English translation. Notice how al-Razi presents his objection concisely, followed by the response.
Pay attention to the key terms: المساواة (equality), المفاوتة (inequality), الدور (circularity).
2
Identify the two parts
The passage has a clear two-part structure: first al-Razi's objection (circularity), then the response from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya. Locate where each part begins.
The response starts with 'وذكر في المباحث المشرقية أنه يمكن أن يجاب عنه...'
3
Understand why circularity is avoided
The response argues that equality and inequality are sensed directly, while quantity is not sensed alone but only together with the quantified. The intellect then distinguishes the concepts. Since the sensible equality is self-evident, it can define the intelligible quantity without circularity. Reflect on this reasoning.
This resolves the objection because the sensible does not depend on the intelligible for its definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام الرازي: لا يمكن تعريف الكم بالمساواة والمفاوتة لأن المساواة لا تعرف إلا بأنها اتحاد في الكم فيلزم الدور وذكر في المباحث المشرقية أنه يمكن أن يجاب عنه بأنَّ المساواة واللامساواة مما يدرك بالحس والكم لا يناله الحس مفرداً، بل إنما يناله مع المتكمم تناولاً واحداً ثم إنَّ العقل يجتهد في تمييز أحد المفهومين عن الآخر فلهذا يمكن تعريف ذلك المعقول بهذا المحسوس يعني وهذا المحسوس مستغن عن التعريف وإمكان أخذه في تعريفه لا يقتضي توقف معرفته عليه
Imam al-Razi said: It is not possible to define quantity by equality and inequality because equality is known only as being a union in quantity, so circularity follows. And it was mentioned in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya that it is possible to answer him that equality and inequality are perceived by the senses, and the senses do not perceive quantity alone, but rather they perceive it together with the quantified in one act, then the intellect strives to distinguish one of the two concepts from the other. So for this reason, it is possible to define that intelligible by this sensible, meaning: this sensible is independent of definition, and the possibility of taking it in its definition does not necessitate that its knowledge depends on it.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. Al-Razi objects that defining quantity by equality/inequality is circular because equality is defined as unity in quantity. The response from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya states that equality and inequality are perceived by the senses directly, whereas quantity is not sensed alone but together with the quantified in a single act. The intellect then distinguishes between the two concepts. Therefore, the sensible equality/inequality can define the intelligible quantity without circularity, since the sensible is self-evident and does not require prior knowledge of quantity.

Key Terms
المساواة
المفاوتة
الدور
الحس
الكم
المتكمم
المباحث المشرقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the response is still circular because it uses equality to define quantity, but the response argues that sensation provides a non-circular starting point.
Correction: The sensible equality is self-evident and does not depend on the concept of quantity for its definition, thus breaking the circle.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why is the definition of quantity by equality/inequality not circular?
A) Because equality is defined by quantity
B) Because equality and inequality are sensed directly, without needing prior knowledge of quantity
C) Because quantity is sensed alone
D) Because the intellect does not distinguish between concepts

Key Takeaway
The response resolves the circularity by grounding the definition of quantity in sense perception of equality and inequality, which are self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend your most cherished beliefs, do you rely on circular justifications that ultimately assume what you want to prove? Can you identify a sensible, undeniable foundation that grounds those beliefs, or are you trapped in intellectual circularity?
?
The intellect distinguishes concepts from wholes. In your own life, have you mistaken a complex experience for a simple concept? What role did your senses play in that process, and when did you need to step back and let your intellect clarify?
?
Al-Razi’s objection exposes the risk of definitions that presuppose themselves. Are there any areas of your faith or practice where you accept definitions that hide circular reasoning? How will you break that cycle by anchoring yourself in something self-evident—perhaps an undeniable truth of your own experience?

Defining Quantity by Divisibility: Critique and Correction
Critique of divisibility definition and manuscript correction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the problem with defining quantity by ordinary division, the solution using hypothetical division, and the manuscript correction that clarifies the terminology.

Core Idea
The definition of quantity hinges on which division we accept.

This passage delves into a technical debate in Islamic logic about the definition of quantity. The author presents a critique, then a resolution, and finally a manuscript correction that resolves an apparent inconsistency. Follow along to see how careful terminology and textual accuracy matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Read the opening statement: defining quantity as acceptance of division is problematic because ordinary division applies only to continuous quantity. Understand why this is a criticism.
The critique assumes a particular understanding of division.
2
Understand the resolution
Note that the correct view uses 'hypothetical division' (al-qisma al-fardiyya), which applies to both continuous and discrete quantities. This resolves the problem.
Hypothetical division is a theoretical division, not actual separation.
3
Examine the misinterpretation
The author criticizes al-Iji's interpretation that Imam al-Razi meant 'separative division' (al-qisma al-infikakiyya), because continuous quantity does not accept actual separation.
This shows the importance of precise terminology.
4
Learn the manuscript correction
Al-Iji himself corrected his manuscript from 'al-munfasil' to 'al-muttasil' to align with al-Razi. Some scholars using the uncorrected copy formed a wrong opinion.
Textual criticism can resolve apparent contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولا) يمكن أيضاً تعريف الكم ( بقبول القسمة لأنه يختص بالمتصل منه ) قد عرفت وجه الاختصاص بالمتصل وعدم تناوله للمنفصل بالقيد الذي زيد في مفهوم القسمة الوهمية كما صرح به في المباحث وأشير إليه في الملخص وعرفت أيضاً أن الصواب عدم اعتبار ذلك القيد وأنَّ القسمة الفرضية تتناول الكم بقسميه معاً فيجوز تعريفه : بقبول هذه القسمة وأما توجيه المصنف كلام الإمام بقوله : ( كأنه أخذ القسمة الانفكاكية) فليس بشيء إذ قد تبين آنفاً أنَّ الكم المتصل لا يقبل القسمة الانفكاكية وقد قرره الإمام في كتابيه تقريراً واضحاً فكيف يتصور اختصاص قبول القسمة الانفكاكية بالكم المتصل ؟ واعلم أنه وقع في نسخة المتن التي بخط المصنف لفظة المنفصل فغيرها بخطه إلى المتصل لأنه الموافق لكلام الإمام في كتابيه . فمنهم من لم ينتبه ؛ لذلك فبنى الكلام على النسخة الأولى فادعى أنَّ القسمة الانفكاكية مختصة بالمنفصل فاستبصر أنت بما حققناه لك ولا تكن من الخابطين
(And it is) also not possible to define quantity (by acceptance of division because it is specific to the continuous) — you already know the reason for its specificity to the continuous and that it does not include the discrete because of the condition that was added to the concept of imaginary division, as stated in al-Mabahith and alluded to in al-Mulakhkhas. And you also know that the correct view is not to consider that condition, and that hypothetical division encompasses both types of quantity together, so it is permissible to define it as acceptance of this division. As for the author's interpretation of Imam's words by his saying: (as if he took the separative division) it is nothing, since it has just become clear that continuous quantity does not accept separative division, and the Imam established it clearly in his two books, so how can one imagine that acceptance of separative division is specific to continuous quantity? And know that in the copy of the text in the author's handwriting, the word 'al-munfasil' (discrete) occurred, so he changed it with his pen to 'al-muttasil' (continuous) because it agrees with the Imam's statement in his two books. Some did not pay attention to that, so they built their discussion on the first copy and claimed that separative division is specific to the discrete. So see clearly with what we have verified for you and do not be among the confounded.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents a critique and a resolution. It first states that defining quantity by divisibility is problematic because divisibility is specific to continuous quantity, not discrete, due to a condition added to the concept of imaginary division. However, the correct view is that this condition should not be considered, and hypothetical division applies to both continuous and discrete quantity, so the definition is valid. The commentator then criticizes the author's (al-Iji's) interpretation of Imam al-Razi's words as referring to separative division (al-qisma al-infikakiyya), because continuous quantity does not accept separative division. Then a manuscript correction is noted: the word 'al-munfasil' was changed to 'al-muttasil' in the author's own handwriting to agree with al-Razi's statements. Some scholars who used the uncorrected copy mistakenly thought separative division is specific to discrete quantity. The passage ends with an admonition to follow the correct understanding.

Key Terms
القسمة
المتصل
المنفصل

Defining Quantity by the Unit: Farabi and Ibn Sina
Quantity can be defined by the property of having a unit that measures it, as per Farabi and Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Farabi and Ibn Sina defined quantity by the property of having a unit, and why this definition avoids circularity.

Core Idea
Quantity is best defined by its relation to a measuring unit.

In this passage, the author presents an alternative definition of quantity based on the notion of a 'unit' (al-'adad) that measures it. This definition, attributed to al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, is highlighted as all-encompassing and non-circular. Let's explore how this definition works and why it is significant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall idea.
2
Identify the key claim
Notice the claim that quantity can be defined by having a unit. What is the rationale given?
3
Understand the property
The passage says the property is 'all-encompassing' and knowledge of it does not depend on knowledge of quantity. Why is that important?
This avoids circularity.
4
Examine the definition by Farabi and Ibn Sina
They define quantity as that in which something can exist as a single measuring unit. Note the inclusion of actual or potential existence.
This covers both discrete and continuous quantities.
5
Reflect on examples
Think of examples where the unit is actual (e.g., number of apples) and potential (e.g., a line segment that can be divided).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(بل) يمكن تعريف الكم ( بوجود العاد) فإنه الخاصة الشاملة للكم ولا تتوقف معرفتها على معرفته ولذلك عرفه الفارابي وابن سينا بأنه الذي يمكن أن يوجد فيه شيء يكون واحداً عاداً له سواء كان موجوداً بالفعل أو بالقوة .
(Rather) it is possible to define quantity (by the existence of a unit) for it is the all-encompassing property of quantity, and knowledge of it does not depend on knowledge of quantity. Therefore, al-Farabi and Ibn Sina defined it as that in which something can exist that is a single unit (one) that measures it, whether it exists actually or potentially.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a definition. It states that quantity can be defined by the property of having a unit (al-'adad) that measures it. This property is universal to all quantities (both continuous and discrete). Importantly, knowledge of this property does not depend on prior knowledge of quantity, so the definition is not circular. It then cites al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, who defined quantity as that in which something can exist that is a single measuring unit, either actually (like the number of discrete items) or potentially (like the potential divisions of a continuous magnitude).

Key Takeaway

Defining Continuous Quantity and the Common Boundary
Defines continuous quantity and explains its characteristic common boundary with a distributive meeting of parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of continuous quantity as a magnitude whose parts meet at a common boundary, and the distributive nature of this meeting.

Core Idea
Continuous quantity is defined by parts meeting at a common boundary, but each pair meets separately.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif introduces the concept of continuous quantity, a fundamental division in the study of quantity. The author defines it by the presence of a common boundary between parts, using the example of a line. However, careful attention is needed to avoid misunderstanding how parts meet—they do not all share one boundary but rather each adjacent pair meets at its own boundary. This passage clarifies that subtle point and explains why the definition uses 'parts' instead of 'two parts'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice the mixture of original text and commentary in the Arabic.
2
Identify the definition and example
Locate the definition of continuous quantity: the presence of a common boundary between parts. See how the line example illustrates that any point can be an end and a beginning.
3
Understand the clarification on meeting
The commentator emphasizes that the meeting of parts is distributive, not collective. Each pair of adjacent parts shares its own boundary. This is a key nuance.
The wrong belief is that all parts meet at one point.
4
Reflect on the choice of words
The author used 'parts' (plural) instead of 'two parts' because every magnitude can have many parts. This shows the general applicability of the definition.
This connects to the idea that division can continue indefinitely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الثاني : الكم المتصل والفرضي ] ( في أقسامه فإن كان بين أجزائه حد مشترك فهو ( الكم (المتصل) كالمقدار ) فإن أي جزء من الخط فرض فهو نهاية لجزء وبداية لجزء باعتبار ونهاية للجزأين باعتبار) آخر وبداية لهما باعتبار ثالث، فإنَّ ذلك يختلف (بحسب ما يبتدأ منه فرضاً ) وتوضيحه أنَّ الكم هو الذي يمكن لذاته أن يفرض فيه شيء غير شيء، فالذي يمكن أن يفرض فيه أجزاء تتلاقى على حد واحد مشترك بين جزأين منها، فهو المتصل والحد المشترك، هو ذو وضع بين مقدارين يكون هو بعينه نهاية لأحدهما . قوله : ( تتلاقى على حد واحد إلخ كان الظاهر أن يقول فالذي يمكن أن يفرض فيه جزءان متلاقيان على حد واحد فهو المتصل وإلا فهو المنفصل إلا أنه اعتبر الأجزاء إشارة إلى الأجزاء إشارة إلى أن جميع الأجزاء المفروضة كذلك. وليس المراد بتلاقي الأجزاء أنها بطريق الاجتماع تتلاقى على حد واحد، فإنه محال بل على سبيل التوزيع بأن يتلاقى اثنان على حد وآخران على حد آخر وهكذا بقرينة قوله بين جزان منها . قوله : (فإن أي جزء أي بين الأجزاء كما نقل عنه فلا يرد الحد المفروض على الجزء الأخير من الخط مثلاً. قوله : ( أجزاء تتلاقى إنما قال: أجزاء ولم يقل جزأين مع أنّه المناسب بقوله : يمكن أن يفرض فيه شيء غير شيء بناء على أنَّ كل مقدار يمكن أن يفرض فيه أجزاء كما أشار إليه سابقاً بقوله : ولايزال كذلك أبداً ثم تلاقي الأجزاء ليس باعتبار أن كلاً من الأجزاء الثلاثة تلاقي الأخيرين مثلا بل باعتبار أنَّ هذا الجزء يلاقي ذلك الجزء على حد واحد وذلك الجزء يلاقي من الجانب الآخر الجزء الآخر على حد واحد أيضاً.
[Second Observing Post: Continuous and Discrete Quantity] (Regarding its divisions, if between its parts there is a common boundary, then it is (continuous quantity) like magnitude. For any part of the line that is assumed, it is an end for one part and a beginning for another part in one respect, and an end for the two parts in another respect, and a beginning for them in a third respect, for that differs according to what is taken as the starting point in the assumption. And its clarification is that quantity is that in which, by its essence, something other than itself can be assumed. So that in which parts can be assumed that meet on a single common boundary between two of its parts is continuous, and the common boundary is something having position between two magnitudes, being exactly the end of one of them. He said: (meeting on a single common boundary etc.) Apparently he should have said: 'So that in which two parts can be assumed meeting on a single common boundary is continuous, otherwise it is discrete,' but he considered the parts to indicate that all assumed parts are like that. And the intent by the meeting of the parts is not that they meet collectively on a single common boundary, for that is impossible, but rather in a distributive manner: two parts meet on one boundary, and two others on another boundary, and so on, as indicated by his saying 'between two of them.' He said: (For any part) meaning between the parts, as transmitted from him, so the assumed boundary does not apply to the last part of the line, for example. He said: (parts that meet) he said 'parts' and did not say 'two parts' even though that would be appropriate because of his saying 'something other than itself can be assumed,' based on the fact that every magnitude can have parts assumed in it, as he previously indicated by his saying 'and it continues like that always.' Then the meeting of the parts is not because each of the three parts meets the other two, for example, but because this part meets that part on one boundary, and that part meets the other part from the other side on another boundary also.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage defines continuous quantity. It states that if between the parts of a quantity there is a common boundary (had mush tarak), then it is continuous, like a line. The example of a line illustrates that any assumed point can be an end for one part and a beginning for another, varying according to the direction of assumption. The commentary clarifies that the definition should be understood as the possibility of assuming parts that meet on a common boundary; this meeting is distributive, not collective — meaning each pair of adjacent parts shares a boundary, not all parts sharing the same boundary. The commentator explains why the author said 'parts' instead of 'two parts', because every magnitude can be divided into many parts. Finally, the meeting is not that one part meets two others, but that each adjacent pair meets at a distinct boundary.

Key Terms
المتصل
المنفصل
الحد المشترك
التلاقي
المقدار
الخط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All parts of a continuous quantity meet at a single common boundary.
Correction: The parts meet distributively: each adjacent pair meets at its own boundary.

Checkpoint

A) All parts meet at one common boundary
B) Each pair of adjacent parts meets at its own boundary
C) Parts do not meet at all
D) Only the first and last parts meet

Key Takeaway
Continuous quantity is defined by a common boundary between parts, but the meeting is distributive, with each adjacent pair sharing its own boundary.

Defining Quantity by the Measurer
Al-Isfahani defines quantity (al-kamm) by the existence of the measurer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how quantity (al-kamm) is defined relationally through the existence of a measurer, and why this definition is comprehensive and non-circular.

Core Idea
Quantity is defined not by itself but by the measurer that applies to it.

This passage presents a clever definition of quantity (al-kamm) that avoids the circularity of defining it by its essence. Instead, it defines quantity by the existence of a measurer (al-‘add), a universal property that applies to all kinds of quantity. The great philosophers al-Farabi and Ibn Sina adopt this approach, offering a definition that works whether the measurer is actual or potential.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the key term 'وجود العاد' (existence of the measurer).
The passage is short, so pay attention to each phrase.
2
Understand the universal property
The passage says that 'the existence of the measurer' is a universal property (خاصة شاملة) of quantity. This means it applies to all types of quantity without exception.
This property does not require prior knowledge of quantity itself, which avoids circularity.
3
Examine the definition by al-Farabi and Ibn Sina
They define quantity as 'that in which something can exist that is a single measurer for it, whether actually or potentially.' Identify the two key conditions: the measurer is single and can be actual or potential.
This definition highlights the relational nature of quantity.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why defining quantity by its own essence is problematic and how this relational definition solves that problem. This aligns with the broader theme of defining things by their properties.
This definition is foundational for logic and ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل يمكن تعريف الكم بوجود العاد فإنه الخاصة الشاملة للكم ولا تتوقف معرفتها على معرفته ولذلك عرفه الفارابي وابن سينا بأنه الذي يمكن أن يوجد فيه شيء يكون واحداً عاداً له سواء كان موجوداً بالفعل أو بالقوة.
Rather, quantity can be defined by the existence of the measurer, for it is the universal property of quantity, and its knowledge does not depend on knowledge of it. Therefore al-Farabi and Ibn Sina defined it as that in which something can exist that is a single measurer for it, whether it exists actually or potentially.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage offers a definition of quantity (al-kamm). The author states that quantity can be defined by 'the existence of the measurer' (wujud al-‘add). This is a property that encompasses all types of quantity and does not presuppose knowledge of quantity itself. He then cites al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, who defined quantity as that in which something can exist that serves as a single measurer for it, whether that measurer exists in actuality or only in potentiality.

Key Terms
الكم (al-kamm)
العد (al-‘add)
الخاصة الشاملة (al-khāṣṣa al-shāmila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quantity must be defined by its intrinsic essence, such as 'extension' or 'discreteness'.
Correction: The passage shows that quantity can be defined relationally by the existence of a measurer, which is a universal property and avoids circularity.

B) As that in which something can exist that is a single measurer for it

Key Takeaway
Quantity is defined by the existence of a measurer, a universal property that applies to all quantities and avoids circularity.
